Arguments Against Using Reason in Theology
Author presents arguments against rational opinion in theology, citing al-Bazdawi and Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that abstract reason cannot be used as an independent source in theology, and how error in theological ijtihad differs from error in legal ijtihad.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot cause truth in theology without Revelation's authority.

Sanusi warns against relying on abstract reason in matters of creed. He draws an analogy from fiqh, where using opinion is already condemned, to theology where it is even worse. He then cites al-Bazdawi on the limits of reason and distinguishes the severe consequences of error in theology from the excusable mistakes in fiqh. This lesson unpacks these arguments and their Quranic and Hadith foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own logic come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has a cumulative argument structure with multiple sub-arguments.
2
Identify the two main arguments against reason
The passage presents two explicit arguments: (1) an analogy from fiqh to theology, and (2) a warning against listening to philosophers who turn from revelation. Find these and understand their connection.
The first argument uses a 'fortiori' reasoning from fiqh to theology.
3
Understand the distinction between error in theology and fiqh
The author distinguishes error in this chapter (theology) from error in ijtihad in furu' (fiqh). In theology, error is not excused and carries no reward, while in fiqh it may be excused and even rewarded. This is key to the author's overall position.
This distinction is crucial for evaluating the ijtihad of Ahl al-Bid'a vs Ahl al-Sunna.
4
Reflect on the scriptural evidence
The author cites Quran 6:68, 2:26, 17:82, and a hadith about the Quran being an argument for or against. Consider how these support the argument against relying on abstract reason.
The Quranic verses are used to emphasize the danger of turning away from revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها: أن القول بالرأي والعقل المجرد في الفقه والشريعة بدعة وضلالة فأولى أن يكون ذلك في علم التوحيد والصفات بدعةً وضلالة، فقد قال فخر الإسلام علي البزدوي في أصول الفقه: إنه لم يرد في الشرع دليل على أن العقل موجب ولا يجوز أن يكون موجبا وعلة بدون الشرع، إذ العلل موضوعات الشرع، وليس إلى العباد ذلك؛ لأنه ينزع أي يسوق إلى الشركة، فمن جعله موجبا بلا دليل شرعا فقد جاوز حد العباد وتعدّى عن حدّ الشرع على وجه العناد. ومنها: الإصغاء إلى كلام الحكماء وأتباعهم من السفهاء حيث أعرضوا عن الآيات النازلة من السماء وخاضوا مع الجهلاء الذين يظنّ فيهم أنهم العُقلاء والعُلماء وقد نبه الله تعالى على ذلك في كتابه حيث قال: ﴿وَإِذَا رَأَيْتَ الَّذِينَ يَخُوضُونَ فِي آيَاتِنَا فَأَعْرِضْ عَنْهُمْ حَتَّى يَخُوضُوا فِي حَدِيثٍ غَيْرِهِ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٦٨] فإن معنى الآية يشملهم إذ العبرة بعموم المبنى لا بخصوص السبب لذلك المعنى، والتأويلات الباطلة والتحريفات العاطلة قد تكون كفراً وقد تكون فسقاً وقد تكون معصيةً وقد تكون خطأ والخطأ في هذا الباب غير معفو ومرفوع بخلاف الخطأ في اجتهاد الفروع حيث لا وزر هنالك بل أجر يترتب على ذلك. وبهذا تبين وجه الفرق بين اجتهاد أهل البدعة مع اختلافهم وبين اجتهاد أهل السنة مع اختلافهم ويشير إليه قوله تعالى: يُضِلُّ بِهِ كَثِيرًا وَ يَهْدِي بِهِ كَثِيرًا ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٦] وَنُنَزِّلُ مِنَ الْقُرْآنِ مَا ﴿ هُوَ شِفَاءٌ وَرَحْمَةٌ لِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَلَا يَزِيدُ الظَّلِمِينَ إِلَّا خَسَارًا ﴾ [الإسراء: ۸۲] وفي الحديث: ((القرآن حجة لك أو عليك)).
Among them: that holding opinion and abstract reason in fiqh and Sharia is innovation and misguidance, so more appropriately that is innovation and misguidance in the science of tawhid and attributes. For Fakhr al-Islam Ali al-Bazdawi said in Usul al-Fiqh: No evidence came in the Sharia that reason is a cause, and it is not permissible for it to be a cause and a 'illa without Sharia, indeed the causes are subject to Sharia, and that is not up to the servants; because it leads to partnership. So whoever makes it a cause without a Sharia proof has indeed exceeded the limit of servants and transgressed the limit of Sharia out of obstinacy. And among them: listening to the speech of the philosophers and their followers from the foolish, as they turned away from the verses sent down from heaven and plunged with the ignorant who are thought to be the intelligent and the learned. And Allah the Exalted alerted to that in His Book where He said: {And when you see those who plunge into Our verses, then turn away from them until they plunge into other discourse} [Al-An'am: 68]. Indeed the meaning of the verse includes them, since consideration is by the generality of the wording not by the specificity of the reason for that meaning. And the false interpretations and empty distortions may be kufr, may be fisq, may be sin, and may be error. And error in this chapter is not forgiven and raised? versus error in ijtihad of furu' where there is no sin there but rather reward accrues from that. And by this the difference between the ijtihad of Ahl al-Bid'a with their differences and the ijtihad of Ahl al-Sunna with their differences is clarified. And it is indicated by His statement: {He misguides many thereby and guides many thereby} [Al-Baqara: 26] and {And We send down of the Qur'an that which is healing and mercy for the believers and does not increase the wrongdoers except loss} [Al-Isra: 82] and in the hadith: "The Qur'an is an argument for you or against you."
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents several arguments against the use of ra'y (opinion) and 'aql mujarrad (abstract reason) in theology. The author first argues by analogy: if such reason in fiqh and Sharia is bid'a and dhalala, it is even more so in 'ilm al-tawhid wa al-sifat. He then quotes al-Bazdawi from Usul al-Fiqh, stating that the Sharia does not make reason a cause (mujib) or 'illa without evidence; those who claim otherwise exceed their bounds. The second argument warns against listening to philosophers who turn from revelation and engage in false interpretation. Citing Quran 6:68, the author notes the verse's generality covers them. He then classifies errors from false interpretations as kufr, fisq, sin, or error. Importantly, he distinguishes error in theology from error in ijtihad in furu': in theology, error is not excused and does not receive reward, whereas in fiqh, it may be excused and even rewarded. This clarifies the difference between the ijtihad of Ahl al-Bid'a and Ahl al-Sunna, supported by Quran 2:26, 17:82, and the hadith that the Quran is an argument for or against a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that error in theological ijtihad is excused like in fiqh.
Correction: The author insists that error in theology is not excused and does not carry reward, unlike error in fiqh where reward may be obtained.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes error in theology from error in fiqh (furu') ijtihad?
A) Error in theology is always kufr, while error in fiqh is always fisq.
B) Error in theology is not excused and carries no reward, while error in fiqh may be excused and even rewarded.
C) Error in theology is less serious than error in fiqh.
D) There is no distinction; both are equally excused.

Key Takeaway
Using abstract reason as an independent source in theology leads to innovation and misguidance, and theological errors are not excused like those in fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a belief or action by your own reasoning even when a clear Quranic verse or authentic hadith contradicted it? What does that reveal about where your ultimate authority lies?
?
If theological error is not forgiven like error in fiqh, how seriously do you take learning correct 'aqeedah? Are you willing to submit your intellect to revelation even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
The author warns against 'listening to the philosophers' who turn away from revelation. Who are the 'philosophers' of our time—influential speakers, books, or ideas—that subtly lead you to rely on human reason over divine guidance? Will you cut them off?

The Obligation to Follow the Prophet and the Sufficiency of Revelation
Opening metaphor and obligation to follow the Prophet with verse 4:65.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Prophet's teaching is sufficient and obligatory to follow, and that those who turn away from his judgment are condemned.

Core Idea
The Prophet's teaching is complete and must be followed without seeking other judgments.

Sanusi begins with a powerful metaphor: the Quran is like the Nile—life-giving for believers and destructive for those who reject it. He then establishes the absolute duty to follow the Prophet, citing a Qur'anic verse that makes true faith conditional on accepting his judgment without reservation. This lesson introduces the foundational principle that no part of religion can be judged by external sources.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening metaphor
Read the first sentence carefully. The Nile metaphor contrasts how the same source can be beneficial or harmful depending on one's state.
This metaphor sets the tone: revelation is clear, but reception varies.
2
Identify the obligation to follow the Prophet
The author states it is obligatory upon all Muslims to follow the Master of the Messengers. Notice he links this to consistency with previous prophets and the clear discernment of the Book.
3
Reflect on the Qur'anic verse
Verse 4:65 is quoted. The author emphasizes that Allah swore by Himself that true faith requires making the Prophet the judge and submitting fully without discomfort.
This verse is the core proof for the sufficiency of prophetic judgment.
4
Understand the description of hypocrites
The author explains that hypocrites desire judgment from other sources and turn away when called to Allah and His Messenger. This sets up the refutation of syncretism that follows.
This behavior is contrasted with true faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو كبحر النيل ماء للمحبوبين ودماء للمحجوبين، فالواجب على المسلمين أجمعين اتباع سيد المرسلين المطابق لما جاء به عقيدة سائر النبيين وعين التبيين للكتاب المبين. وقد بين سبحانه أمره وعظم شأنه وقدره حيث أقسم بنفسه فقال: ﴿ فَلَا وَرَبِّكَ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ حَتَّى يُحَكِّمُوكَ فِيمَا شَجَرَ بَيْنَهُمْ ثُمَّ لَا يَجِدُوا فِي أَنْفُسِهِمْ حَرَجًا مِمَّا قَضَيْتَ وَيُسَلِّمُوا تَسْلِيمًا ﴾ [النساء: ٦٥] . وأخبر أن المنافقين يريدون أن يتحاكموا إلى غيره، وأنهم إذا دعوا إلى الله أي كتابه ورسوله أي حكمه صدوا عنه صدودا أي أعرضوا عنه إعراضا بعيدا وأنهم يزعمون أنهم إنما أرادوا إحساناً وتوفيقاً وإيقاناً وتحقيقاً كما يقوله كثير من المتكلمين والمتفلسفة وغيرهم: إنما نريد الأشياء بالجمع بين كلام الأنبياء والحكماء. وكما يقوله كثير من المبتدعة من المتنسكة : إنما نريد الإحسان بالجمع بين الإيمان والإيقان والتوفيق بين الشريعة والطريقة والحقيقة، ويدسون فيها دسائس مذاهبهم الباطلة ومشاربهم العاطلة من الحلول والاتحاد والاتصال والانفصال ودعوى الوجود المطلق وأن الموجودات بأسرها عين الحق ويتوهمون أنهم في مقام الجمعية، والحال أنهم في حال التفرقة وضلال الزندقة. وكما يتفوه كثير من المتملكة والمتأمرة : إنما نريد الإحسان بالسياسة الحسنة البديعة والتوفيق بينهما وبين الشريعة. طلب أن يحكم في شيء من أمر الدين غير ما ثبت عن النبي الأمين صلى الله عليه وسلم، ويظن أن ذلك مستحسن في باب اليقين، وأن ذلك جامع بين ما جاء به الرسول ما يخالفه من المعقول فله نصيب من ذلك وحرام عليه الترقي إلى ما هنالك، إذ ما جاء به الرسول كاف شاف كامل تبيّن فيه حكم كل حق وباطل قال الله تعالى: وَلَا تَلْبِسُو الْحَقَّ بِالْبَاطِلِ وَتَكْتُبُوا الْحَقَّ وَأَنْتُمْ تَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ [البقرة : ٤٢] . (1) صحيح مسلم كتاب الطهارة، باب فضل الوضوء، ص ١٤٠، حدیث: ٢٢٣.
For it is like the Nile River, water for the beloved and blood for the veiled. So it is obligatory upon all Muslims to follow the Master of the Messengers, consistent with what he brought, the creed of all prophets and the clear discernment of the Clear Book. And He the Exalted clarified His matter and magnified His status and measure when He swore by Himself saying: {But no, by your Lord, they do not believe until they make you judge concerning that which they dispute among themselves, then find within themselves no discomfort from what you judged and submit fully} [An-Nisa: 65]. And He informed that the hypocrites want to be judged by other than him, and that when they are called to Allah (i.e. His Book) and His Messenger (i.e. His judgment), they turn away in aversion, a far aversion. And they claim that they only intended goodness, harmony, certainty, and verification, as said by many of the mutakallimun, philosophers, and others: "We only want things by combining the speech of the prophets and the philosophers." And as said by many of the innovators among the ascetics: "We only want goodness by combining faith and certainty and reconciling between Sharia, Tariqa, and Haqiqa." And they insert therein the corruptions of their false doctrines and empty ways, such as hulul, ittihad, ittisal, infisal, and the claim of absolute existence and that all existents are exactly the Truth (al-Haqq), and they imagine themselves in the station of union, while they are in a state of separation and the misguidance of zandaqa. And as uttered by many of the rulers and commanders: "We only want goodness through good and innovative politics and reconciliation between them and the Sharia." Whoever seeks to judge in any matter of religion other than what is established from the Prophet al-Amin, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and thinks that is commendable in the matter of certainty, and that it combines what the Messenger brought with what opposes it from the intellect, then he has a portion of that, and it is forbidden for him to ascend to what is there. For what the Messenger brought is sufficient, healing, complete; therein is clarified the judgment of every truth and falsehood. Allah said: {And do not mix truth with falsehood and conceal the truth while you know} [Al-Baqara: 42]. (1) Sahih Muslim, Kitab al-Tahara, Bab Fadl al-Wudu', p. 140, hadith 223.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage emphasizes the completeness and sufficiency of the Prophet's message. The author begins with a metaphor: the Quran is like the Nile River—water for the beloved (believers) and blood for the veiled (disbelievers). He then asserts the obligation to follow the Prophet, whose teaching is consistent with all previous prophets and is the clear discernment of the revealed Book. Citing Quran 4:65, the author notes that true faith requires accepting the Prophet's judgment without discomfort. He then describes how hypocrites desire to be judged by other sources and turn away from Allah and His Messenger, claiming to seek goodness and harmony. The author criticizes three groups: (1) mutakallimun (theologians) and philosophers who combine prophetic and philosophical teachings; (2) innovators among ascetics who mix Sharia, Tariqa (path), and Haqiqa (reality), and introduce dangerous doctrines like hulul (incarnation), ittihad (union), and the claim of absolute existence; (3) rulers who try to reconcile politics with Sharia. The author then declares that whoever seeks judgment from other than what the Prophet established, thinking to combine revelation with contradictory reason, is in error. Ultimately, what the Messenger brought is kafin (sufficient), shafin (healing), and kamil (complete), distinguishing all truth from falsehood. He concludes with Quran 2:42, forbidding the mixing of truth with falsehood. The footnote cites a hadith on the virtue of wudu, likely supporting the earlier hadith about the Quran as argument.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for true faith as stated in Qur'an 4:65?
A) To pray and fast regularly
B) To make the Prophet judge in disputes and submit fully without discomfort
C) To combine revelation with philosophy
D) To follow the consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
True faith requires completely accepting the Prophet's judgment without seeking alternative sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a disagreement or dispute, do you instinctively seek the judgment of the Prophet (through the Qur'an and Sunnah) or do you prefer your own opinion, custom, or the advice of others? What would it take to make the Prophet's judgment your first and only resort?
?
Think of a ruling from the Prophet that you find difficult or uncomfortable to accept. Why does it cause discomfort? Will you ask Allah to remove that unease and submit fully, or will you continue to let it fester? Your iman depends on your answer.
?
The hypocrites turned away when called to Allah and His Messenger. In what areas of your life are you still turning away—whether by ignoring a clear command, rationalizing disobedience, or seeking validation elsewhere? Repent now and resolve to surrender entirely.

Refuting Syncretism: Combining Revelation with Philosophy or Mysticism
Refutation of those who combine revelation with philosophy or mysticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of those who try to combine prophetic teaching with philosophy or mystical practices, and the dangerous doctrines that result.

Core Idea
Combining revelation with philosophy or mysticism leads to heresy, not harmony.

Sanusi now turns to specific groups who claim to seek goodness and certainty by synthesizing revelation with other sources. He first addresses theologians and philosophers who want to combine the words of prophets and sages. Then he targets innovative ascetics who blend Sharia, Tariqa, and Haqiqa, and in doing so, introduce corrupt doctrines like incarnation, union, and absolute existence. This lesson exposes the error of syncretism and the danger of veiling truth with falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Read the claims of the theologians and philosophers
Notice how they claim to combine the speech of prophets and philosophers. Understand that they present this as an effort to achieve goodness, harmony, and certainty.
This is the first group refuted.
2
Read the claims of the innovative ascetics
They claim to reconcile Sharia, Tariqa, and Haqiqa, and believe they are combining faith and certainty.
3
Identify the corrupt doctrines they introduce
The author lists specific heretical beliefs such as hulul (incarnation), ittihad (union), ittisal, infisal, absolute existence, and that all existents are exactly the Truth. These are consequences of their syncretism.
These doctrines are explicitly condemned.
4
Recognize the author's judgment
The author states that although they imagine themselves in a station of union (jami'iyya), they are in fact in a state of separation (tafriqa) and the misguidance of zandaqa (heresy).
This is a strong condemnation.

Source Text

فهو كبحر النيل ماء للمحبوبين ودماء للمحجوبين، فالواجب على المسلمين أجمعين اتباع سيد المرسلين المطابق لما جاء به عقيدة سائر النبيين وعين التبيين للكتاب المبين. وقد بين سبحانه أمره وعظم شأنه وقدره حيث أقسم بنفسه فقال: ﴿ فَلَا وَرَبِّكَ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ حَتَّى يُحَكِّمُوكَ فِيمَا شَجَرَ بَيْنَهُمْ ثُمَّ لَا يَجِدُوا فِي أَنْفُسِهِمْ حَرَجًا مِمَّا قَضَيْتَ وَيُسَلِّمُوا تَسْلِيمًا ﴾ [النساء: ٦٥] . وأخبر أن المنافقين يريدون أن يتحاكموا إلى غيره، وأنهم إذا دعوا إلى الله أي كتابه ورسوله أي حكمه صدوا عنه صدودا أي أعرضوا عنه إعراضا بعيدا وأنهم يزعمون أنهم إنما أرادوا إحساناً وتوفيقاً وإيقاناً وتحقيقاً كما يقوله كثير من المتكلمين والمتفلسفة وغيرهم: إنما نريد الأشياء بالجمع بين كلام الأنبياء والحكماء. وكما يقوله كثير من المبتدعة من المتنسكة : إنما نريد الإحسان بالجمع بين الإيمان والإيقان والتوفيق بين الشريعة والطريقة والحقيقة، ويدسون فيها دسائس مذاهبهم الباطلة ومشاربهم العاطلة من الحلول والاتحاد والاتصال والانفصال ودعوى الوجود المطلق وأن الموجودات بأسرها عين الحق ويتوهمون أنهم في مقام الجمعية، والحال أنهم في حال التفرقة وضلال الزندقة. وكما يتفوه كثير من المتملكة والمتأمرة : إنما نريد الإحسان بالسياسة الحسنة البديعة والتوفيق بينهما وبين الشريعة. طلب أن يحكم في شيء من أمر الدين غير ما ثبت عن النبي الأمين صلى الله عليه وسلم، ويظن أن ذلك مستحسن في باب اليقين، وأن ذلك جامع بين ما جاء به الرسول ما يخالفه من المعقول فله نصيب من ذلك وحرام عليه الترقي إلى ما هنالك، إذ ما جاء به الرسول كاف شاف كامل تبيّن فيه حكم كل حق وباطل قال الله تعالى: وَلَا تَلْبِسُو الْحَقَّ بِالْبَاطِلِ وَتَكْتُبُوا الْحَقَّ وَأَنْتُمْ تَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ [البقرة : ٤٢] . What corrupt doctrine do the innovative ascetics introduce according to the passage?
A) The idea that prophets and philosophers agree
B) The claim of absolute existence and that all existents are the Truth
C) The need for political governance

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt pressured to reconcile Islamic teachings with modern philosophies or spiritual trends to make them more acceptable to yourself or others? What does this passage say about the outcome of such attempts? Ask Allah for sincerity and steadfastness.
?
In your spiritual journey, have you ever prioritized emotional experiences or mystical states over the clear rulings of Sharia? The author warns that this can lead to heresy. Examine your heart: are there any practices you follow that claim to be 'spiritual' but contradict the Sunnah? Resolve to align yourself fully with the Qur'an and Sunnah.
?
If you were accused of holding heretical beliefs, would you be able to defend yourself with evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah? This passage shows how easily one can fall into zandaqa while thinking they are on a higher path. Commit to studying sound aqeedah and avoiding any form of syncretism.

The Danger of Mixing Politics with Revelation and the Finality of Prophetic Guidance
Refutation of political syncretism and final declaration of the Prophet's teaching as sufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that mixing political innovation with Sharia is forbidden, and that the Prophet's teaching is complete, sufficient, and healing, making all other sources unnecessary.

Core Idea
The Prophet's teaching is complete and sufficient; adding to it or mixing it with politics is forbidden.

Sanusi now addresses a third group: rulers and those in authority who claim to improve society by combining good politics with Sharia. He then makes a general statement that anyone who seeks judgment on religious matters from a source other than the Prophet is in error. Finally, he concludes with the powerful declaration that what the Prophet brought is sufficient, healing, and complete, clarifying every truth and falsehood. The lesson ends with a Qur'anic prohibition against mixing truth with falsehood, cementing the author's stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the rulers' claim
Rulers claim to seek goodness through 'good and innovative politics' and reconciliation between politics and Sharia.
This is the third group refuted.
2
Understand the general principle
The author states that whoever seeks to judge any religious matter by something other than what the Prophet established, thinking it combines revelation with reason, has a share of that error and it is forbidden for him to ascend to that level.
This applies to all groups, not just rulers.
3
Grasp the final declaration of sufficiency
The author declares that what the Messenger brought is sufficient (kafi), healing (shafi), and complete (kamil), and it clarifies the judgment of every truth and falsehood.
This is the climax of the passage.
4
Reflect on the concluding verse
The Qur'anic verse 2:42 forbids mixing truth with falsehood and concealing the truth knowingly.
This verse reinforces the prohibition of syncretism.

?

?

Can you identify an area in your life—your career, family, or community—where you have justified compromising a clear Islamic teaching because you thought it would lead to a greater good? How does Allah's command 'do not mix truth with falsehood' confront that compromise right now?
?
If the Prophet's teaching clarifies every truth and falsehood, what are you relying on when you feel uncertain about a moral or religious issue? Are you seeking answers from the revelation first, or are you more comfortable turning to culture, opinion, or personal reasoning? What change will you make this week to prioritize the source that is already complete?

beginner
6 min
The Sufficiency of Quran and Sunnah
The author argues from Quran and Sunnah sufficiency, citing verses and Tahawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran and Sunnah are considered completely sufficient for the fundamentals of religion, and why personal opinions, spiritual tastes, and ecstatic states are not authoritative sources.

Core Idea
The Quran and Sunnah are complete; no personal opinion or spiritual taste adds to religion.

This passage makes a powerful claim: Allah has not left us in need of human opinions, spiritual experiences, or personal interpretations to know the fundamentals of our faith. The Quran and authentic Sunnah are declared perfect and sufficient. To support this, the author cites several Quranic verses and concludes with Imam Tahawi’s famous statement from his creed, which echoes the rejection of subjective interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the main claim
Read the author's opening statement that the Sunnah explains and confirms the Quran, and that Allah has not made us reliant on personal opinions, spiritual tastes, or ecstatic states.
Notice the phrase 'رأي فلان وذوق فلان ووجد فلان' – these are rejected as sources for fundamentals.
2
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Reflect on each of the four verses cited (Al-Ma'idah 3, Ibrahim 52, Al-Ankabut 51, Al-Hashr 7). Consider how each verse supports the sufficiency and completeness of revelation.
The verse from Al-Ma'idah is especially clear: 'Today I have perfected for you your religion.'
3
Connect to Tahawi's creed
Read Imam Tahawi's statement and see how it reinforces the rejection of personal opinion (ra'y) and whims (ahwa') in matters of creed.
Tahawi's phrase 'لا ندخل في ذلك متأولين بآرائنا ولا متوهمين بأهوائنا' is a direct echo.
4
Apply the lesson
Recognize that this principle guards against innovation (bid'ah) and ensures unity in core beliefs. Consider how this applies to contemporary debates about religious authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا السنة تأتي مبينة ومقررة لما دل عليه القرآن فلم يحوجنا ربنا سبحانه وتعالى إلى رأي فلان وذوق فلان ووجد فلان في أصول ديننا، ولذا نجد من خالف الكتاب والسنة مختلفين مضطربين، بل قال الله تعالى: ﴿ الْيَوْمَ أَكْمَلْتُ لَكُمْ دِينَكُمْ وَأَتْمَمْتُ عَلَيْكُمْ نِعْمَتِي وَرَضِيتُ لَكُمُ الْإِسْلَامَ دِينًا ﴾ [المائدة:3] فلا نحتاج في تكميله إلى أمر خارج عن الكتاب والسنة، كما قال الله تعالى: هَٰذَا بَلَاغٌ لِلنَّاسِ [إبراهيم: ٥٢] وقال الله تعالى: ﴿أَوَلَمْ يَكْفِهِمْ أَنَا أَنْزَلْنَا عَلَيْكَ الْكِتَابَ يُتْلَى عَلَيْهِمْ ﴾ [العنكبوت: ٥١] وقال الله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا آتَاكُمُ الرَّسُولُ فَخُذُوهُ وَمَا نَهَاكُمْ عَنْهُ فَانْتَهُوا ﴾ [الحشر: ٧]، وإلى هذا المعنى أشار الطحاوي بقوله في أوّل عقيدته: «لا ندخل في ذلك متأولين بآرائنا ولا متوهمين بأهوائنا ، فإنه ما سلم في دينه إلا دينه إلا من سلّمه الله عزوجل».
And likewise the Sunnah comes explaining and confirming what the Quran indicates, so our Lord, glorified and exalted, has not made us need the opinion of so-and-so, nor the taste of so-and-so, nor the ecstasy of so-and-so in the fundamentals of our religion. Hence we find those who contradict the Book and the Sunnah to be disunited and confused. Rather, Allah Almighty said: 'Today I have perfected for you your religion and completed My favor upon you and have approved for you Islam as a religion' [Al-Ma'idah:3]. So we do not need in its completion something external to the Book and the Sunnah. As Allah Almighty said: 'This is a proclamation to mankind' [Ibrahim:52], and Allah Almighty said: 'Is it not sufficient for them that We have sent down to you the Book which is recited to them?' [Al-Ankabut:51], and Allah Almighty said: 'And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it, and whatever he forbids you, abstain from it' [Al-Hashr:7]. And to this meaning Imam Tahawi alluded in his saying at the beginning of his creed: 'We do not enter into that by interpreting with our opinions nor by conjecturing with our whims, for indeed, he who is safe in his religion is only safe by the safety granted by Allah, the Mighty and Majestic.'
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the complete sufficiency of the Quran and Sunnah in matters of religion, particularly in the fundamentals of faith (usul al-din). The author states that the Sunnah (prophetic tradition) serves to explain and confirm the Quran, and that Allah has not made us reliant on personal opinions, spiritual tastes (dhawq), or mystical states (wajd) of individuals — these are not sources for fundamental beliefs. Those who contradict the revealed sources end up divided and confused. The author then quotes several Quranic verses to support this: (1) The verse from Al-Ma'idah 3 declaring the perfection of religion, implying that no addition is needed. (2) The verse from Ibrahim 52 calling the Quran a proclamation to mankind. (3) The verse from Al-Ankabut 51 asking if the Book sent down is not sufficient. (4) The verse from Al-Hashr 7 commanding to take what the Messenger gives and avoid what he forbids. These verses together establish that the Quran and Sunnah are complete and sufficient. The author then cites Imam Tahawi's creed, which explicitly states that in matters of belief, we do not rely on personal interpretation (ta'wil) or whims (ahwa'), but rather on divine guidance. The final phrase from Tahawi emphasizes that safety in religion comes only from Allah's protection.

Key Terms
الكتاب
السنة
أصول الدين
الرأي
الذوق
الوجد
الطحاوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that personal spiritual experiences (dhawq, wajd) or scholarly opinions can supplement or even override the Quran and Sunnah in matters of fundamental belief.
Correction: The passage clearly states that Allah has not made us need such things; the Quran and Sunnah are complete and sufficient. Any deviation from them leads to division and confusion.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do those who contradict the Book and Sunnah become divided and confused?
A) Because they follow different madhhabs
B) Because they rely on personal opinions, tastes, and ecstasies instead of revelation
C) Because they do not study Arabic
D) Because they reject the Sunnah entirely

Key Takeaway
The Quran and Sunnah are complete and sufficient for all matters of fundamental belief; no personal opinion or spiritual experience can add to or change them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Imam Tahawi says safety in religion comes only from Allah, not from our opinions. Do you truly feel secure in your faith because of Allah's guidance, or are you actually trusting in your own understanding? How can you shift your reliance entirely to revelation?

Beliefs Must Be from Revelation
Argues that beliefs must be taken from Shari'ah even if reason can prove them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why beliefs must be taken from Shari'ah even when reason can independently prove them, and why rational proofs require scriptural validation to be considered authoritative.

Core Idea
Reason can prove God, but only revelation makes that belief valid.

This lesson establishes a key methodological principle in Islamic theology: although human reason can arrive at certain truths about God, the ultimate source for beliefs must be revelation. The author explains that rational proofs for God's existence and attributes, while valid in themselves, only count as sound Islamic theology when they are checked against the Book and Sunnah. Without this check, they become mere philosophy and carry no weight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own wording sink in.
Notice the phrase 'الشرع الذي هو الأصل' – the Shari'ah is the foundation.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states clearly that beliefs must be taken from Shari'ah even if the intellect can be independent. This is the central thesis.
3
Understand the nuance
The author then clarifies that rational proofs for God's attributes do not depend on scripture in their own essence, but their validity in the Islamic context depends on conformity to scripture. This is a key distinction.
The word 'الاعتداد بها' means 'being considered valid'.
4
Consider the analogy
The author warns that without scriptural conformity, theology becomes like the divine science of philosophers, which is not authoritative. Reflect on what this implies about the relationship between reason and revelation.
5
Reflect on the misconception
This passage does not reject reason; rather, it subordinates rational proofs to revelation for acceptance. Think about how this complements both sources.
The common misunderstanding is that reason and revelation are opposed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العقائد يجب أن تؤخذ من الشرع الذي هو الأصل وإن كانت ممّا يستقل فيه العقل وإلا فعلم إثبات الصانع وعلمه وقدرته لا تتوقف من حيث ذاتها على الكتاب والسنة، ولكنها تتوقف عليهما من حيث الاعتداد بها؛ لأن هذه المباحث إذا لم يعتبر مطابقتها للكتاب والسنة كانت بمنزلة العلم الإلهي للفلاسفة، فحينئذ لا عبرة بها على ما ذكره المحققون.
Then beliefs must be taken from the Shari'ah which is the foundation, even if they are matters in which the intellect can be independent. Otherwise, the knowledge of proving the Creator, His knowledge and power, does not depend in essence on the Book and Sunnah, but it depends on them in terms of being considered valid. Because if these investigations are not considered in accordance with the Book and Sunnah, they become like the divine science of the philosophers, and then they have no weight, according to what the verifiers have mentioned.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a methodological principle in kalam. The author states that although the intellect can arrive at certain truths (like the existence of God) independently, the final authority for beliefs must be revelation (Shari'ah). He explains that rational proofs for the Creator's attributes are not inherently dependent on Quran and hadith (they can be known by reason), but their validity and acceptance in the Islamic context depend on their conformity to revealed texts. If rational arguments are not checked against scripture, they resemble the metaphysical speculations of philosophers and are not considered authoritative. This is a classic Ash'ari/Maturidi position that reason is valid but must be guided by revelation.

Key Terms
العقائد
الشرع
العقل
الكتاب والسنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reason and revelation are opposed and that Islamic theology rejects rational proofs.
Correction: The passage shows that reason is valid but must be subordinated to revelation for its conclusions to be considered authoritative. They are complementary, not opposed.

Checkpoint

A) Pure reason
B) The Shari'ah
C) Philosophical speculation
D) Personal intuition

Key Takeaway
Even rational proofs for God's existence and attributes must be validated by revelation to be accepted as sound Islamic belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Imam Abu Hanifa's Deliberate Wording for the Last Day
Explains why Imam Abu Hanifa specified resurrection after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Abu Hanifa specifically mentioned resurrection after death instead of a general faith, and how it includes barzakh and maqif.

Core Idea
Precision in creedal wording ensures comprehensive belief.

This lesson examines a subtle but important methodological choice by Imam Abu Hanifa. Instead of relying on the general faith summarized in the shahada, he specifically names 'resurrection after death' as a distinct article of faith. The author of the sharh suggests this was to follow the Prophet's example while also including the intermediate states of barzakh and the standing. Understanding this helps us appreciate the precision required in creedal formulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author begins with 'perhaps' (لعل), indicating a reasoned suggestion.
The passage explains a deliberate choice.
2
Identify the two options
Imam Abu Hanifa could have used general faith (contained in the shahada) or specified resurrection. See why he chose the latter.
3
Reflect on the inclusion
Think about how specifying resurrection by its beginning (resurrection after death) includes barzakh and maqif. This shows careful theological scope.
Connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولعل الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله عدل عن الإيمان الإجمالي المشتمل عليه كلمتا الشهادة تبعا للسان له صلى الله عليه وسلم حيث أجاب سؤال جبرائيل عليه السلام عن الإيمان بهذا المقدار من إلا أن الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله عبّر عن اليوم الآخر بمبدئه من البعث بعد الموت ليشمل حال البرزخ والموقف.
And perhaps the Great Imam, may Allah have mercy on him, turned away from the general faith contained in the two words of the testimony, following the tongue of him, peace be upon him, when he answered Gabriel's question about faith with this amount, except that the Great Imam, may Allah have mercy on him, expressed the Last Day by its beginning, which is resurrection after death, to include the state of the barzakh and the standing (maqif).
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage offers a likely reason (لعل) why Imam Abu Hanifa, in his creed (Fiqh al-Akbar), did not merely mention the general faith covered by the shahada but instead specified 'resurrection after death' as a distinct article of faith. The author suggests that the Imam followed the prophetic example when the Prophet answered Gabriel's question about faith. However, the Imam expressed 'the Last Day' by its beginning, namely resurrection after death, in order to encompass the intermediate state (barzakh) and the standing (maqif). This choice of wording is deliberate to include these eschatological states.

Correction: The author explains it is meant to include barzakh and maqif, which are not explicitly covered by the general term 'Last Day' alone.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa deliberately specified resurrection after death to ensure creedal coverage of all eschatological states following the prophetic model.

Iqna'iyya vs Qat'iyya: The Probative Nature of the Verse
Al-Taftazānī's claim and refutation by leading scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the verse is a persuasive (iqna'iyya) or certain (qat'iyya) proof, and the grammatical basis for its certainty.

Core Idea
The verse's certainty hinges on the logical grammar of 'law'.

In this passage, the author addresses a crucial methodological challenge: If the verse from the previous proof is merely persuasive (iqna'iyya), then the entire argument would be weakened. Al-Taftazānī argued that the implication is only customary, not necessary. However, leading scholars like al-Ghazālī rejected this, insisting the verse is a certain proof. The author also clarifies the grammatical function of 'law' to show that the verse offers a timeless logical deduction, not a past counterfactual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from Taftazānī's claim to the refutation to the grammatical note.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents al-Taftazānī's view that the verse is iqna'iyya (persuasive) and the muḥaqqiqūn's view that it is qaṭ'iyya (certain). Mark how each side supports its claim.
The muḥaqqiqūn include al-Ghazālī, Ibn al-Humām, and al-Bayḍāwī.
3
Understand the grammatical note
The author explains that 'law' in the verse is not a past conditional but indicates a general logical condition. Reflect on how this strengthens the certainty of the proof.
This ties back to the idea that the argument is not based on custom but on logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول العلامة التفتازاني: الآية حجة إقناعية أي يظن في أول الأمر أنها حجة ويزول ذلك عند تحقق المعرفة والملازمة عادية على ما هو اللائق بالخطابيات، فإن العادة جارية بوجود التمانع والتغالب عند تعدد الحاكم على ما يشير إليه قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَعَلَا بَعْضُهُمْ [المؤمنون: ٩١] فالمحققون كالغزالي وابن الهمام والبيضاوي ما قنعوا بالإقناعية وجعلوها من الحقائق القطعية، بل قيل : يكفر قائلها والمسألة مستوفاة في الكتب الكلامية. ثم اعلم أن "لو" في هذه الآية ليست لانتفاء الثاني في الماضي بسبب انتفاء الأول كما هو أصل اللغة بل للاستدلال بانتفاء الجزاء على انتفاء الشرط من غير دلالة على تعين زمان، فإنه قد يستعمل بهذا المعنى في بعض المبنى.
As for the statement of the eminent al-Taftazānī: 'The verse is a persuasive (iqnāʿiyya) proof, meaning that one thinks at first glance that it is a proof, but that disappears upon verification; and the implication is customary, as is appropriate for rhetorical arguments, for the norm (ʿāda) is for mutual hindrance and conflict to occur when there is a multiplicity of rulers, as is indicated by His statement, the Exalted: 'They would then have sought a way over each other' [Q. 23:91],' but the verified scholars (al-muḥaqqiqūn) like al-Ghazālī, Ibn al-Humām, and al-Bayḍāwī were not content with its being persuasive; rather they made it one of the certain truths (ḥaqāʾiq qaṭʿiyya). In fact it is said that the one who claims that would become a disbeliever (yakfur). And the matter is thoroughly covered in the kalām books. Then know that 'law' (if) in this verse is not for the negation of the second [clause] in the past due to the negation of the first, as is the original usage in the language, but rather for demonstrating the non-occurrence of the consequence (jazāʾ) based on the non-occurrence of the condition, without indicating a specific time. For indeed it is sometimes used in this sense in [some] constructions.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage addresses the logical status of the verse used in the previous proof. Al-Taftazānī claimed that it is only a persuasive (iqnāʿiyya) argument, not a decisive proof. He argued that the implication of mutual hindrance from multiplicity is based on customary experience (ʿāda), not logical necessity. The author then reports that prominent scholars such as al-Ghazālī, Ibn al-Humām, and al-Bayḍāwī rejected this downgrading; they held the verse to be a certain proof (ḥaqīqa qaṭʿiyya). In fact, some said that the one who claims it is iqna'iyya becomes a disbeliever. The author then adds a grammatical note: the word 'law' in the verse is not used in its usual 'past counterfactual' sense, but rather to indicate a general logical condition without temporal specification, which supports its universal applicability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An iqna'iyya proof is not a valid proof at all.
Correction: Al-Taftazānī still considered it a proof, but not a decisive one.

Checkpoint

B) A persuasive proof (iqnāʿiyya)

Key Takeaway
The verse is a certain proof (qaṭʿiyya) because the grammar of 'law' indicates a logical condition, not a past counterfactual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Rational Terms for Allah: The Ijma Argument
The author answers an objection about applying rational terms to Allah, citing ijma as a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of scholars (ijma) justifies using rational theological terms for Allah, even when those terms are not explicitly found in the Quran or Sunnah.

Core Idea
Consensus (ijma) is a valid religious proof that legitimizes rational terminology in theology.

This passage addresses a common objection: why do theologians use terms like 'necessary' and 'eternal' when these words don't appear in the primary revealed texts? The answer is surprisingly simple: the collective agreement of the scholarly community (ijma) itself is a recognized source of Islamic law and doctrine. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how ijma bridges reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the concise structure: a conditional objection followed by a direct answer.
The answer is only two words in Arabic: بالإجماع (by consensus).
2
Identify the objection's basis
The objector asks how terms not found in the Sharia (i.e., Quran and Sunnah) can be applied to Allah. The assumption is that all theological language must be scripturally authorized.
3
Understand the answer: ijma as proof
The author responds with 'by consensus (ijma)', and adds that ijma is among the legal proofs (adilla shar'iyya). This means the agreement of qualified scholars is itself a source of legitimacy.
Ijma is the third source of Islamic law after Quran and Sunnah.
4
Reflect on the implication
If the community of scholars agrees on a term, it becomes binding, even if the term is not in the primary texts. This allows theology to use rational concepts within an Islamic framework.
5
Test your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to confirm you can identify the key proof in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: كيف صح إطلاق الموجود والواجب والقديم ونحو ذلك مما لم يرد به الشرع؟ قلنا: بالإجماع وهو من الأدلة الشرعية.
If it is said: How is it permissible to apply the terms 'existent', 'necessary', 'eternal', and the like, which have not been transmitted in the Sharia? We say: By consensus (ijma), and it is among the legal proofs.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The objector questions the legitimacy of using philosophical or rational terms such as 'existent' (mawjud), 'necessary' (wajib), and 'eternal' (qadim) for Allah, since these specific words are not found in the Quran or hadith. The answer is simple: by consensus (ijma) of the scholars. Ijma is one of the four sources of Islamic law and doctrine. Thus, the community's agreement on the permissibility of such terms provides a valid basis, making it unnecessary for each term to be individually revealed.

Key Terms
إطلاق
الموجود
الواجب
القديم
الإجماع
أدلة شرعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only terms explicitly mentioned in the Quran or authentic hadith can be used to describe Allah.
Correction: While scripture is primary, the consensus (ijma) of scholars is also a valid proof. Terms agreed upon by the scholarly community are legitimate, even if not found verbatim in the primary texts.

Checkpoint
What proof does the author provide for using terms like 'wajib' for Allah even though they are not explicitly in the revealed texts?
A) Qiyas (analogy) to revealed terms.
B) The consensus of the Muslim community (ijma).
C) The inherent necessity of reason.
D) A direct command from the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Consensus (ijma) is a valid legal proof that allows the use of rational terms for Allah, even when those terms are not scripturally specified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah using terms like 'The Wise' or 'The Loving', do you ever pause to wonder if you are speaking correctly? How does knowing that the scholars' consensus (ijma) legitimizes such rational descriptions change your confidence? Are you willing to trust the collective tradition of Islamic scholarship over your own personal hesitations?
?
Think of a time you dismissed a theological term about Allah because it wasn't familiar or didn't appear in the Quran. Was your reluctance rooted in piety or in ignorance? How can you humbly submit to the ijma of the scholars and still remain vigilant against innovation?
?
This lesson argues that ijma is a valid proof. Do you truly live as if the consensus of the righteous predecessors matters? Or do you privately believe you know better than centuries of scholarship? Let this question unsettle you until you seek the company of those who honor authority.

The Superiority of Submission in Acts of Worship
The author explains that submission in devotional acts is superior due to lack of self-interest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why submission in devotional acts of worship is considered superior, and how humility about knowledge is a virtue exemplified by early Muslims.

Core Idea
Submission without self-interest is the highest form of worship.

In this passage, the author concludes a discussion on the role of intellect versus submission in matters of faith. He asserts that devotional acts (al-'ibādāt al-ta'abbudiyyah) are superior precisely because they leave no room for ego. To reinforce this, he cites a Quranic verse, a prophetic saying, a wise maxim, and two powerful anecdotes from Ali ibn Abi Talib and Abu Yusuf. This lesson will walk you through his argument and its supporting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice how the author builds his case from the general claim to specific examples.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا حظ للنفس فيها' – no share for the ego.
2
Identify the main argument
The author's central thesis is that submission (inqiyād) in devotional acts is better and more complete than other acts. Identify the reason he gives: the absence of any personal gain.
3
Examine the supporting proofs
The passage provides five proofs: a Quranic verse, a hadith, a saying, and two stories. For each, ask: How does it support the idea of humility and submission?
The stories of Ali and Abu Yusuf are especially vivid – they openly say 'I do not know' despite their high status.
4
Reflect on the lesson's implication
Consider how admitting ignorance can be a form of worship. How does this challenge the common desire to appear knowledgeable?
This connects to the core idea that submission requires ego suppression.

And it is said: 'Inability to attain comprehension is comprehension.' And it was asked of Ali (may God be pleased with him), and he said: 'I do not know,' while he was on the pulpit. He said: 'I ascended according to my knowledge of things, and if I ascended according to my ignorance I would reach the sky.' It was said to him: 'You take such and such from the treasury and are unable to explain this situation?' He said: 'Yes, I take money according to my knowledge, and if I took according to my ignorance I would exhaust all wealth.'

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory conclusion based on the previous discussion. The author uses the word 'صمود' (steadfastness/acknowledgment) to indicate that the first group relied on intellect, while the better path is submission. He argues that acts of devotional worship are superior because they involve no egoistic share, but pure obedience to the divine command. To support this, he cites the Quranic verse that mankind has been given but little knowledge, and a hadith that saying 'I do not know' is half of knowledge. He also quotes a saying that the inability to fully comprehend is itself a form of comprehension. Then he narrates two stories: one about Ali ibn Abi Talib (ra) who openly said 'I do not know' on the pulpit, and another about Abu Yusuf who similarly admitted his ignorance despite receiving a salary. These stories illustrate the virtue of acknowledging one's limited knowledge and the importance of humility.

The Virtue of Submission and Admitting Ignorance
Author argues for superiority of devotional acts and virtue of saying 'I don't know'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why devotional acts are considered superior in Islamic spirituality and why acknowledging ignorance is a sign of true knowledge.

Core Idea
True submission is better than intellectual pride; admitting 'I don't know' is half of knowledge.

In this lesson, we explore a key principle of Islamic spirituality: devotional acts that involve pure submission to Allah are superior to those driven by personal reasoning. The author supports this with a Quranic verse and sayings emphasizing human limitation, and then illustrates the virtue of admitting ignorance through two powerful anecdotes. This teaching challenges the common notion that intellectual achievement is the highest form of worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from principle to evidence to stories.
The passage contains a verse, two sayings, and two anecdotes – all supporting one central idea.
2
Identify the main principle
Find where the author states that submission in devotional acts is better and more complete. Understand why: because there is no share for the soul, only pure obedience to Allah's command.
The phrase 'الانقياد في العبادات التعبدية أفضل وأكمل' is the thesis.
3
Reflect on the evidence
Look at the Quranic verse and the two sayings. Each reinforces human limitation: knowledge is little, 'I don't know' is half of knowledge, and realizing our inability to grasp is a form of perception.
These are not just quotes; they are building a case for humility.
4
Learn from the anecdotes
Consider the stories of Ali and Abu Yusuf. Both were highly learned yet publicly said 'I do not know.' Their responses to criticism show that true scholars acknowledge their limits.
Notice how Ali's reply ties his knowledge to his ascension – he ascends only according to his knowledge, not his ignorance.
5
Summarize the lesson
Now that you have read and reflected, formulate the key takeaway: devotional submission is superior because it involves complete trust, and admitting ignorance is a mark of wisdom, not deficiency.

Attributing Evil to God: General vs. Specific Permission
The author reports the Salaf's agreement on general attribution and some's prohibition of specific evil attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Salaf agreed on attributing all things to God in general but forbade specifying evil acts as willed by God, and the principle of adab in theological speech.

Core Idea
General attribution is permitted; specific attribution of evil is forbidden out of reverence.

This lesson focuses on a key adab principle in Islamic theology: how to speak about God's will regarding evil. The early Muslim scholars (Salaf) agreed that everything happens by God's will, yet they refrained from explicitly saying 'God wills disbelief' out of respect and to avoid misunderstanding. Let's examine their reasoning and the analogy they used.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the distinction between 'general' and 'specific' attribution.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two positions: (1) general permission to attribute all things to God, and (2) some scholars forbidding specific attribution of evil. Find the exact phrases that describe each.
Look for 'اتفقوا على جواز إسناد الكل' and 'منهم من منع التفصيل'.
3
Understand the reasoning
Why did some scholars forbid specific attribution? The text gives two reasons: because it might suggest disbelief (īhām al-kufr) and out of observance of proper etiquette (ri'āyat al-adab). Ask yourself how these reasons apply.
This connects the rule to deeper principles of theology and reverence.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The passage draws an analogy with 'Creator of things' vs. 'Creator of filth'. Think about why we avoid the second phrase even though it is technically true. How does this analogy illustrate the principle?
The analogy shows that truth alone is not sufficient; manner of expression matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا القول هو المعروف عن السلف، وقد اتفقوا على جواز إسناد الكل إليه سبحانه جملة، فيقال جميع الكائنات مرادة الله، ومنهم من منع التفصيل فقال: لا يقال إنه يريد الكفر والظلم والفسق لإيهامه الكفر ولرعاية الأدب سبحانه كما يقال خالق الأشياء، ولا يقال خالق القاذورات.
And this statement is the well-known one from the Salaf, and they agreed on the permissibility of attributing everything to Him, may He be exalted, in general terms, so it is said: 'All things are willed by God.' And among them is one who forbade specification and said: 'It should not be said that He wills disbelief, injustice, and sin, because it suggests disbelief and out of observance of proper etiquette towards Him, just as it is said: Creator of things, but it is not said: Creator of filth.'
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a methodological clarification regarding theological language. The author states that earlier pious ancestors (Salaf) agreed that it is permissible to attribute everything to God in a general sense—one may say 'All things are willed by God.' However, some scholars forbade specifying particular evils (like disbelief, injustice, sin) as being directly willed by God. The reason is twofold: first, it might suggest (īhām) that God approves of such evil, which would imply disbelief; second, it is a matter of proper adab (etiquette) towards God. An analogy is given: we say 'Creator of things' but not 'Creator of filth,' even though He creates everything. The lesson teaches that while God's will is universal, speech about God must observe reverence and avoid phrases that could be misinterpreted as attributing evil to Him in a way that suggests moral approval.

Key Terms
السلف (the Salaf)
إسناد الكل (attribution of all things)
جملة (generally)
تفصيل (specifically)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since God creates all, we may freely say 'God wills evil' without qualification.
Correction: While it is true in a general sense, specific attribution is avoided out of reverence and to prevent misunderstanding that God approves of evil.

Checkpoint

A) Because God does not actually will disbelief
B) Because it might imply that God approves of disbelief and to maintain proper etiquette
C) Because disbelief is not part of God's will
D) Because the Salaf disagreed on whether God wills all things

Key Takeaway
The Salaf agreed that all things are willed by God, but they avoided specifying evil acts as willed by God out of proper etiquette and to prevent misunderstanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a personal tragedy or witness a grave injustice, what words come to your mind about God's role in it? Do you ever feel the urge to blame God directly for the evil? How does this lesson call you to discipline your tongue and your heart in that moment?

Think of a specific time you spoke about a calamity as 'God's will' in a way that implied disapproval or complaint. How can you now rephrase that thought with the reverence the Salaf taught, acknowledging God's universal will without specifying evil?
?
If someone close to you said, 'God willed that I commit this sin,' how would you gently correct them using the teaching of this passage? What would you feel in your own soul if you heard yourself say such a thing?

The Three Categories of Works
The author enumerates the three categories of works and lists the divine attributes involved in the obligatory category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of human works (obligatory, virtue, disobedience) as defined in classical Sunni kalam, and the list of divine attributes that pertain to the obligatory category.

Core Idea
All works fall into three moral-legal categories; the obligatory kind involves a chain of divine attributes.

In this passage, the author clarifies the basic moral-legal framework for human actions. He classifies all works into three categories—obligatory, virtue, and disobedience—and then focuses on the obligatory category, listing the divine attributes (such as will, decree, and creation) that are connected to it. This classification is foundational for understanding the relationship between divine predestination and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of works
Read the sentence beginning with 'We affirm that works are three...' to see the three categories and their definitions.
Notice that obligatory includes both belief and action, virtue covers recommended acts, and disobedience covers sinful acts.
2
Identify the attributes for obligatory works
After the categories, the passage lists numerous divine attributes for the obligatory category. Count them and understand each in context.
This list shows the interconnectedness of divine attributes in relation to human obligation.
3
Reflect on the purpose of this enumeration
Ask why the author lists all these attributes specifically for the obligatory category.
This prepares you for the next lesson on the distinction between eternal will and temporal will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن شارحاً حلّ عبارة الإمام على أن الطاعات والمعاصي مفعولات ليخلق، وأن قوله واجبة خبر ما كانت مندوبة، والأولى ما قرّرنا، وعلى عموم معنى الأمر حرّرنا، والمسألة مبسوطة في 'الوصية' حيث قال: نقر بأن الأعمال ثلاثة: فريضة أي اعتقادا وعملا، أو عملا لا اعتقادا ليشمل الواجب وفضيلة أي سنة أو مستحبة أو نافلة ومعصية أي حرام أو مكروه، فالفريضة بأمر الله تعالى ومشيئته ومحبته ورضاه وقضائه وتقديره وإرادته وتوفيقه وتخليقه أي خلق فعله وفق حكمه فهو تفسير لما قبله. وأما قوله وحكمه وعلمه وكتابته في اللوح المحفوظ فظاهر العبارة هو التفرقة بين المشيئة والإرادة، فالمشيئة أزلية في المرتبة الشهودية والإرادة تعلقها بالفعل في الحالة الوجودية، هذا ما سنح لي في هذا المقام، والله تعالى أعلم بمرام الإمام. وكذا الحكم يظهر أنه مستدرك
Then know that a commentator interpreted the Imam's expression as meaning that acts of obedience and disobedience are created things (mafʿūlāt) by His creation, and that His saying 'obligatory' is the predicate of 'what were' (mā kānat) referring to recommended acts, but what we have established is more appropriate, and we have written based on the general meaning of the command. The issue is detailed in 'al-Waṣiyya' where he said: 'We affirm that works are three: obligatory (farīḍa), which is by belief and action, or by action without belief to include the wajib; and virtue (faḍīla), which is sunna, mustaḥabb, or nāfila; and disobedience (maʿṣiya), which is ḥarām or makrūh. The obligatory (farīḍa) is by the command of God Most High, His will (mashīʾa), His love, His pleasure, His decree, His predestination, His desire (irāda), His success-giving (tawfīq), and His creation (takhlīq)—meaning the creation of his action according to His judgment—so it is an interpretation of what preceded. As for His saying 'and His judgment, His knowledge, and His writing in the Preserved Tablet,' the apparent meaning of the expression is the distinction between mashīʾa and irāda: mashīʾa is eternal in the rank of witnessing, and irāda is its attachment to the action in the state of existence. This is what occurred to me in this context, and God knows best the intention of the Imam. Likewise, the judgment appears to be superfluous.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a meta-commentary on the wording of Imam Abu Hanifa (or the author of Fiqh al-Akbar). The author first notes that a commentator (shāriḥ) interpreted the Imam's statement differently: he took 'acts of obedience and disobedience' as 'created things' (mafʿūlāt) and the word 'obligatory' (wājiba) as a predicate referring to recommended actions. The author prefers his own earlier interpretation that he established in the preceding lesson.

He then references another work, 'al-Waṣiyya', where the Imam reportedly classifies all works into three categories: obligatory (farīḍa), which includes belief and action (or action alone to cover wājib); virtue (faḍīla), which includes sunna, mustaḥabb, and nāfila; and disobedience (maʿṣiya), which includes ḥarām and makrūh. For the obligatory category, the attributes of God involved are many: command, will (mashīʾa), love, pleasure, decree, predestination, desire (irāda), success-giving (tawfīq), and creation (takhlīq) of the action according to His judgment.

Finally, the author draws a subtle distinction between two terms for 'will': mashīʾa and irāda. He explains that mashīʾa is eternal and belongs to the rank of witnessing (al-martaba al-shuhūdiyya), while irāda is the attachment (taʿalluq) of that will to the action in the state of existence (al-ḥāla al-wujūdiyya). This distinction is based on the apparent wording of the Imam's phrase 'and His judgment, His knowledge, and His writing in the Preserved Tablet.' He notes that the term 'judgment' (ḥukm) might be redundant (mustadrak).

The lesson is a careful analysis of theological terminology, showing how precision in language helps avoid confusion about God's eternal will and its temporal manifestation.

Key Terms
فريضة (obligatory)
فضيلة (virtue)
معصية (disobedience)
مشيئة (eternal will)
إرادة (temporal will)
توفيق (success-giving)
تخليق (creation of action)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the three categories are just legal rulings without connection to divine attributes.
Correction: The passage shows that especially the obligatory category is deeply linked to many divine attributes, indicating that moral obligation is grounded in God's will and action.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many divine attributes are explicitly listed for the obligatory category?
A) Three
B) Six
C) Nine
D) Twelve

Key Takeaway
Human works are classified into three moral-legal categories, and the obligatory kind is particularly associated with a comprehensive set of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a specific obligatory act you perform regularly (e.g., prayer). What concrete step will you take this week to engage with that act in light of this truth?

?
The phrase 'creation of his action according to His judgment' means your actions are created by Allah. In what area of your life do you need to align your intention and effort more consciously with His judgment? What specific change will you make today to reflect that alignment?

The Distinction Between Mashīʾa and Irāda
The author distinguishes between mashīʾa (eternal will) and irāda (temporal will).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle but important distinction between mashīʾa (eternal will) and irāda (temporal will) as explained by the author.

Core Idea
Mashīʾa is eternal; irāda is that same will attaching to an action in time.

After discussing the three categories of works, the author turns to a finer theological point: the difference between two terms for 'will' used in the Imam's text. He explains that mashīʾa refers to God's eternal will, while irāda refers to the attachment (taʿalluq) of that will to a specific action in existence. This distinction helps clarify how God's unchanging will relates to temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the distinction
Read the sentence starting with 'As for His saying and His judgment...' to identify the two terms and their descriptions.
Notice the key words: mashīʾa is 'azaliyya' (eternal) and in the 'rank of witnessing', while irāda is its attachment to the action in existence.
2
Understand the implications
Why is this distinction important? It shows that God's will is one and eternal, but its application to created events happens in time. This avoids assigning change to God while still affirming that He wills particular events.
Think about how this answers potential objections about God changing His mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن شارحاً حلّ عبارة الإمام على أن الطاعات والمعاصي مفعولات ليخلق، وأن قوله واجبة خبر ما كانت مندوبة، والأولى ما قرّرنا، وعلى عموم معنى الأمر حرّرنا، والمسألة مبسوطة في 'الوصية' حيث قال: نقر بأن الأعمال ثلاثة: فريضة أي اعتقادا وعملا، أو عملا لا اعتقادا ليشمل الواجب وفضيلة أي سنة أو مستحبة أو نافلة ومعصية أي حرام أو مكروه، فالفريضة بأمر الله تعالى ومشيئته ومحبته ورضاه وقضائه وتقديره وإرادته وتوفيقه وتخليقه أي خلق فعله وفق حكمه فهو تفسير لما قبله. وأما قوله وحكمه وعلمه وكتابته في اللوح المحفوظ فظاهر العبارة هو التفرقة بين المشيئة والإرادة، فالمشيئة أزلية في المرتبة الشهودية والإرادة تعلقها بالفعل في الحالة الوجودية، هذا ما سنح لي في هذا المقام، والله تعالى أعلم بمرام الإمام. وكذا الحكم يظهر أنه مستدرك
Then know that a commentator interpreted the Imam's expression as meaning that acts of obedience and disobedience are created things (mafʿūlāt) by His creation, and that His saying 'obligatory' is the predicate of 'what were' (mā kānat) referring to recommended acts, but what we have established is more appropriate, and we have written based on the general meaning of the command. The issue is detailed in 'al-Waṣiyya' where he said: 'We affirm that works are three: obligatory (farīḍa), which is by belief and action, or by action without belief to include the wajib; and virtue (faḍīla), which is sunna, mustaḥabb, or nāfila; and disobedience (maʿṣiya), which is ḥarām or makrūh. The obligatory (farīḍa) is by the command of God Most High, His will (mashīʾa), His love, His pleasure, His decree, His predestination, His desire (irāda), His success-giving (tawfīq), and His creation (takhlīq)—meaning the creation of his action according to His judgment—so it is an interpretation of what preceded. As for His saying 'and His judgment, His knowledge, and His writing in the Preserved Tablet,' the apparent meaning of the expression is the distinction between mashīʾa and irāda: mashīʾa is eternal in the rank of witnessing, and irāda is its attachment to the action in the state of existence. This is what occurred to me in this context, and God knows best the intention of the Imam. Likewise, the judgment appears to be superfluous.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a meta-commentary on the wording of Imam Abu Hanifa (or the author of Fiqh al-Akbar). The author first notes that a commentator (shāriḥ) interpreted the Imam's statement differently: he took 'acts of obedience and disobedience' as 'created things' (mafʿūlāt) and the word 'obligatory' (wājiba) as a predicate referring to recommended actions. The author prefers his own earlier interpretation that he established in the preceding lesson.

He then references another work, 'al-Waṣiyya', where the Imam reportedly classifies all works into three categories: obligatory (farīḍa), which includes belief and action (or action alone to cover wājib); virtue (faḍīla), which includes sunna, mustaḥabb, and nāfila; and disobedience (maʿṣiya), which includes ḥarām and makrūh. For the obligatory category, the attributes of God involved are many: command, will (mashīʾa), love, pleasure, decree, predestination, desire (irāda), success-giving (tawfīq), and creation (takhlīq) of the action according to His judgment.

Finally, the author draws a subtle distinction between two terms for 'will': mashīʾa and irāda. He explains that mashīʾa is eternal and belongs to the rank of witnessing (al-martaba al-shuhūdiyya), while irāda is the attachment (taʿalluq) of that will to the action in the state of existence (al-ḥāla al-wujūdiyya). This distinction is based on the apparent wording of the Imam's phrase 'and His judgment, His knowledge, and His writing in the Preserved Tablet.' He notes that the term 'judgment' (ḥukm) might be redundant (mustadrak).

The lesson is a careful analysis of theological terminology, showing how precision in language helps avoid confusion about God's eternal will and its temporal manifestation.

Key Terms
مشيئة (eternal will)
إرادة (temporal will)
أزلية (eternal)
مرتبة شهودية (rank of witnessing)
حالة وجودية (state of existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think mashīʾa and irāda are synonyms with no difference.
Correction: The passage explicitly distinguishes them: mashīʾa is the eternal will, irāda is the temporal attachment of that will to an action.

Checkpoint

A) Mashīʾa is for good, irāda is for evil
B) Mashīʾa is eternal, irāda is the attachment to the action in existence
C) Mashīʾa is the decree, irāda is the command
D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
Mashīʾa is God's eternal will; irāda is that same will's temporal application to specific actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that your every action becomes attached to God's eternal will at the moment it occurs. When you sinned today, did you consider that your sin was not outside God's will? How does that sobering thought change your resolve to repent and avoid sin? What concrete step will you take tonight to align your action with that awareness?
?
If God's will (mashīʾa) is unchanging and eternal, then your free choices are not random to Him. How does this truth affect the way you approach your next difficult decision? Will you consult God's guidance more earnestly? What specific prayer will you make to seek alignment with His eternal will?
?
The author admits uncertainty ('God knows best the intention of the Imam'). Even scholars recognize theological humility. In your own life, is there an issue of God's will that you have treated as fully resolved? Could you be missing a distinction like this that would bring you closer to understanding? What one question about God's will will you now prayerfully ponder?

The Proof of Abu Bakr's Superiority
Presents the Prophet's appointment of Abu Bakr as imam during illness as evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority according to Ahl al-Sunna, including the Prophet's appointment and the consensus of the Companions.

Core Idea
Prophet's appointment of Abu Bakr to lead prayer is the strongest proof of his excellence.

How do we know that Abu Bakr al-Siddiq was the best of the Companions? The classical Sunni position relies on clear textual evidence from the Prophet's own actions. In this passage, we examine the key proof: the Prophet's decision to put Abu Bakr forward as imam during his final illness, and how this was understood by the senior Companions as a sign of his superiority in both religion and worldly leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the argument builds from a specific action (Prophet's appointment) to a general principle (the best must lead).
2
Identify the primary evidence
Find where the passage states the best evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority. Note that it is the Prophet's appointment of him to lead the people during his illness, both night and day.
This is a direct action, not just a statement.
3
Understand the Companions' inference
The senior Companions reasoned: if the Prophet approved Abu Bakr for the most important religious duty (imam in prayer), then they should approve him for worldly leadership (caliphate).
This shows the link between religious and political leadership.
4
Analyze the supporting consensus and ruling
The passage then cites the consensus of the majority on appointing Abu Bakr as caliph, and a fiqh ruling that in leadership positions (prayer, judge, governor, caliph) the best qualified person must be chosen.
The ruling from 'al-Khulasa' reinforces the principle that the caliph must be the best.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأولى ما يستدل به على أفضلية الصديق في مقام التحقيق نصبه عليه الصلاة والسلام لإمامة الأنام مدة مرضه في الليالي والأيام، ولذا قال أكابر الصحابة رضيه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم لديننا أفلا نرضاه لدنيانا؟» ثم إجماع جمهورهم على نصبه للخلافة ومتابعة غيرهم أيضا في آخر أمرهم، ففي 'الخلاصة': رجلان في الفقه والصلاح سواء إلا أنّ أحدهما أقرأ فقدم أهل المسجد الآخر فقد أساؤوا، وكذا لو قلد القضاء رجلا وهو من أهله وغيره أفضل أهله وغيره أفضل منه وكذا الوالي. وأما الخليفة فليس لهم أن يولوا الخلافة إلا أفضلهم، وهذا في الخلفاء خاصة وعليه إجماع الأمة، انتهى.
And the best thing by which to infer the superiority of al-Siddiq (the Truthful) in the place of verification is his appointment (peace and blessings be upon him) of him to lead the people during his illness in the nights and days. Therefore, the senior Companions said: 'He (peace and blessings be upon him) approved him for our religion, should we not approve him for our worldly affairs?' Then the consensus of the majority of them upon his appointment to the caliphate and the following of others as well at the end of their affair. For in 'al-Khulasa': Two men equal in jurisprudence and righteousness except that one of them is more proficient in recitation, and the people of the mosque put forward the other, then they have done wrong. Likewise, if he appoints a man as judge while he is qualified and another is better, or if he qualifies and another is better, and similarly the governor. As for the caliph, it is not permissible for them to appoint anyone to the caliphate except the best among them. This is specifically for the caliphs, and upon it there is consensus of the Umma. End.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the superiority of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq. It begins by stating that the best evidence in this matter is the Prophet’s appointment of Abu Bakr to lead the congregational prayer during his final illness, both at night and during the day. This act demonstrates that the Prophet considered Abu Bakr the most worthy to lead the community in the most important religious duty. The senior Companions inferred from this that if the Prophet approved him for religion, they should approve him for worldly leadership (i.e., the caliphate). Then the passage cites two arguments: (1) the consensus of the majority of Companions on appointing him to the caliphate, with others eventually following; (2) a ruling from the work 'al-Khulasa' that in prayer leadership, judgeship, and governorship, the best qualified person must be preferred if there is a difference in merit. The ruling concludes that for the caliphate specifically, it is obligatory to appoint the best, and this is agreed upon by the Umma.

Misconception: Some might think the evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority is purely from consensus.
Correction: The passage emphasizes the Prophet's direct appointment during illness as the primary evidence, with consensus as supporting.
