The Introduction of Philosophy into Kalam
Historical note on the integration of philosophy into kalam for refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical reason why Muslim theologians incorporated philosophical methods into kalam.

Core Idea
Philosophy was adopted into kalam to refute it, not to embrace it.

The encounter between Islamic theology and Greek philosophy was not one of blind rejection. As this passage explains, Muslim scholars studied philosophy precisely to defend their faith. They mixed philosophical reasoning with kalam to understand its arguments and then dismantle them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the historical flow.
2
Identify the two key events
The passage mentions two things: translation of philosophy into Arabic, and then the response of the Islamic class. Note how they are connected.
3
Understand the mixed motive
The scholars mixed philosophy with kalam 'to verify its purposes' so they could nullify and refute it. This is a defensive strategy.
4
Consider the long-term consequence
The passage ends by noting that eventually most of philosophy was included in kalam. Reflect on how this changed the field.
5
Reflect on the irony
The tool used to refute philosophy became deeply integrated into the discipline itself. Think about the implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لما نقلت الفلسفة إلى العربية وخاض فيها الطبقة الإسلامية حاولوا الرد على الفلاسفة والحكماء الطبيعية فيما خالفوا فيها الشريعة الحنيفية، فخلطوا بعلم الكلام كثيرا من الفلسفة في مقام المرام ليتحققوا مقاصدها فيتمكنوا من إبطالها وردها وهلم جراً إلى أن أدرجوا فيه معظم

So they mixed much philosophy with ilm al-kalam in the course of the aim to verify its purposes so that they could nullify it and refute it, and so on until they inserted most of
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a historical note on the encounter between kalam and philosophy. The author states that after philosophical works were translated into Arabic, Muslim scholars engaged with them. Their goal was to refute the philosophers and naturalists (al-hukama' al-tabi'iyya) on points where they contradicted the Islamic sharia. To do this effectively, they incorporated a great deal of philosophy into kalam. They did this to fully understand the aims of philosophy so that they could then demolish its arguments and refute them. This process continued until eventually they included most of philosophy within kalam. The passage ends abruptly at the bottom of the page, leaving the sentence incomplete. This illustrates the methodological shift in later kalam where philosophical concepts were integrated as a necessary tool for defending orthodoxy.

Correction: In fact, they studied and adopted its methods to refute its conclusions.

Key Takeaway
Muslim theologians incorporated philosophical methods into kalam not as an acceptance of its worldview, but as a tool for refuting it.

Mixing Philosophy with Kalam and Its Criticism
The author describes how philosophy was mixed into kalām and the resulting criticism from traditionalists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophy entered kalam and why traditionalist scholars criticized it.

Core Idea
Mixing philosophy into kalam earned it blame from scripture-based scholars.

When Greek philosophy was translated into Arabic, Islamic scholars engaged with it to refute its contradictions with the Sharia. However, their deep study led to extensive incorporation of philosophical ideas into kalam, eventually making it almost indistinguishable from philosophy. This blending provoked criticism from scholars who relied solely on the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to grasp the flow: translation of philosophy → engagement → mixing → incorporation → criticism.
Pay attention to the terms for different philosophical sciences.
2
Identify the scholars' original intention
The passage says they mixed philosophy into kalam 'to verify its objectives and thus be able to falsify and refute it.' Understand why they felt the need to study philosophy deeply.
They wanted to refute philosophers, but the method led to over-integration.
3
Understand the criticism
The passage concludes that kalam became blameworthy because it became almost indistinguishable from philosophy, except for including revealed matters (sam'iyyat). Traditionalist scholars considered scripture sufficient for both transmitted and intellectual matters.
This criticism is directed at the later kalam that absorbed philosophy, not early kalam per se.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لما نقلت الفلسفة إلى العربية وخاض فيها الطبقة الإسلامية حاولوا الرد على الفلاسفة والحكماء الطبيعية فيما خالفوا فيها الشريعة الحنيفية، فخلطوا بعلم الكلام كثيرا من الفلسفة في مقام المرام ليتحققوا مقاصدها فيتمكنوا من إبطالها وردها وهلم جراً إلى أن أدرجوا فيه معظم الطبيعيات والإلهيات والرياضيات حتى كاد لا يتميز عن الفلسفيات لولا اشتماله على السمعيات، فصار بهذا الاعتبار مذموما عند العلماء بالكتاب والسنة اللذين يُكتفى بهما في أمر الدين من النقليات والعقليات.
Then when philosophy was translated into Arabic and the Islamic class delved into it, they attempted to refute the philosophers and natural philosophers regarding what they opposed in the Ḥanīfī Sharīʿa. So they mixed with kalām much of philosophy in order to verify its objectives and thus be able to falsify and refute it, and so on until they incorporated into it most of the natural sciences, metaphysics, and mathematics, so much so that it almost became indistinguishable from philosophy except for its inclusion of revealed matters (samʿiyyāt). Thus, on this account, it became blameworthy in the view of the scholars of the Book and the Sunnah, who suffice with both in matters of religion from transmitted (naqliyyāt) and intellectual (ʿaqliyyāt) matters.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a methodological and historical explanation of the relationship between kalām and philosophy. The author states that when Greek philosophy was translated into Arabic, Islamic scholars engaged with it. Their intention was to refute the philosophers (falāsifa) and natural philosophers (ḥukamā' ṭabīʿiyya) wherever they contradicted the divine law (Sharīʿa). To do this effectively, they felt they needed to understand philosophy deeply, so they incorporated many philosophical ideas into kalām, gradually including natural sciences (ṭabīʿiyyāt), metaphysics (ilāhiyyāt), and mathematics (riyāḍiyyāt). As a result, kalām became so philosophical that it was hardly distinguishable from philosophy, except for its inclusion of revealed matters (samʿiyyāt—things known only through revelation, such as prophethood, eschatology, etc.). This mixing led to criticism: scholars who rely solely on the Qur'an and Sunnah considered kalām blameworthy (madhmūm), because they believe the scriptures are sufficient for both transmitted (naqlī) and intellectual (ʿaqlī) aspects of religion, without needing philosophy.

السمعيات
النقليات
العقليات
الطبيعيات
الإلهيات
الرياضيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Islamic scholars approved of philosophical kalām and its methods.
Correction: The passage shows that strict traditionists (scholars of the Book and Sunnah) considered kalām blameworthy because it became nearly indistinguishable from philosophy, and they held scripture sufficient for all matters of religion.

Checkpoint

C) Because it did not use rational arguments
D) Because it was forbidden by Abu Hanifa

Key Takeaway
Kalam became blameworthy to traditionalists because it became almost indistinguishable from philosophy, except for its inclusion of revealed matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever allowed a secondary concern, like winning a debate or proving your intellectual capability, to blur the purity of your commitment to the Book and Sunnah? What practical step will you take this week to ensure your study of Islam remains focused on direct engagement with Allah's revelation rather than on refuting others?
?
The scholars who mixed philosophy into kalam did so with good intentions—to refute falsehood. Yet their method was blamed. Are there areas in your life where you are using methods or sources not from the Quran and Sunnah, even for a 'good cause'? Will you abandon them today, seeking Allah's pleasure alone?

Wiping Over Khuffayn is Sunnah
Explains the sunnah ruling and its dual evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling of wiping over khuffayn and how its proof is derived from both the nearly mutawatir Sunnah and the Quranic qira'at.

Core Idea
Wiping over khuffayn is established by nearly mutawatir Sunnah and supported by Quranic readings.

This lesson examines the ruling on wiping over leather socks (khuffayn) during ablution. The author affirms it as a confirmed sunnah, specifying durations for residents and travelers. He then demonstrates how the Quranic verse on ablution, through its variant readings (qira'at), also hints at this practice, with the Prophet's action removing any ambiguity between the two readings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the ruling, then the evidence.
2
Identify the ruling and duration
The author states wiping over khuffayn is sunnah. Note the specific time limits: one day/night for resident, three days/nights for traveler.
This reflects the standard Hanafi position.
3
Understand the sunnah evidence
The author says this sunnah is 'nearly mutawatir'—meaning it has been transmitted by so many narrators that its authenticity is undeniable.
4
Analyze the Quranic evidence
The verse 5:6 contains the word 'your feet' read in two ways: nasb (washing) and jarr (wiping). These seem contradictory, but the Prophet's practice clarifies: he wiped over khuffayn and washed bare feet.
This shows how Quran and Sunnah work together.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this example demonstrates the integration of Quranic qira'at and Prophetic practice in deriving rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمسح على الخفين سنة، أي للمقيم يوما وليلة وللمسافر ثلاثة أيام بلياليها سنة أي ثابت بالسنة التي كادت أن تكون متواترة، ولا يبعد أن يؤخذ ثبوته من الكتاب أيضاً؛ لأن قوله تعالى: وَأَرْجُلَكُمْ إِلَى الْكَعْبَيْنِ ﴾ [المائدة : ٦] ، قريء بالنصب في السبعة الأظهر في الغسل، والجر الأظهر في المسح وهما متعارضان، وبحسب الحكم مبهمان فبينهما فعل رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم حيث مسحهما حال لبس الخفين وغسلهما عند كشف الرجلين.
And wiping over the khuffayn is sunnah, i.e., for the resident one day and one night, and for the traveler three days and their nights, sunnah, i.e., established by the sunnah which is nearly mutawatir. And it is not far-fetched that its proof is also taken from the Book; because the saying of the Exalted: 'and your feet to the ankles' [al-Ma'idah 6] was recited in the accusative case (nasb) in the seven [qira'at] which is most evident for washing, and in the genitive case (jarr) which is most evident for wiping, and they are contradictory, and according to the ruling they are ambiguous, so the action of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah's peace and blessings be upon him, clarified them, as he wiped on them while wearing the khuffayn and washed them when the feet were uncovered.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on wiping over the khuffayn (leather socks) during ablution. The author states that it is a sunnah, meaning a practice established by the Prophet, and specifies the duration: for a resident one day and one night, for a traveler three days and their nights. He emphasizes that this sunnah is almost mutawatir (widely transmitted). Then he argues that the proof can also be derived from the Quran, specifically from the verse on ablution (5:6): 'and your feet to the ankles.' He notes that this word 'your feet' (أَرْجُلَكُمْ) is recited in two ways in the seven qira'at: in the accusative case (nasb) which indicates washing, and in the genitive case (jarr) which indicates wiping. These two readings appear contradictory, and the legal ruling is ambiguous. The Prophet's practice (Sunnah) clarifies the ambiguity: when wearing khuffayn, he wiped over them; when the feet were bare, he washed them. Thus the Quranic ambiguity is resolved by the Sunnah.

Key Terms

Prayer Behind Any Muslim is Permissible
States the ruling that prayer behind any pious or sinful believer is valid and cites a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prayer behind any Muslim, whether pious or sinful, is permissible based on the Prophet's command.

Core Idea
The Prophet's command makes prayer behind any believer valid, regardless of sinfulness.

This lesson clarifies a key principle in Islamic jurisprudence: the validity of congregational prayer led by any Muslim, even one who commits sins. The author cites a direct prophetic command to establish this ruling. Understanding this principle prevents unnecessary divisions and upholds the unity of the Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a single continuous sentence.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms 'barr' (pious) and 'fajir' (wicked) and understand that they refer to believers, not disbelievers.
The author explicitly clarifies that 'fajir' means a sinful believer.
3
Understand the proof
The author cites the Prophet's command: 'Pray behind every pious and wicked person.' Reflect on how this hadith directly supports the ruling.
This is a clear textual proof from Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا في قوله الله رحمه تعالى: والصلاة خلف كل بر وفاجر أي صالح وطالح من المؤمنين جائزة أي لقوله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم: صلوا خلف كل بر وفاجر.
And likewise in his statement, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And prayer behind every pious and wicked person, i.e., righteous and evil, from the believers is permissible,' that is, because of his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Pray behind every pious and wicked person.'

Commentary
This brief passage confirms the permissibility of praying behind any Muslim, whether he is outwardly pious (al-barr) or sinful (al-fajir). The author clarifies that 'fajir' here means a sinful believer, not a disbeliever. He supports this with the Prophet's command to pray behind every pious and wicked person. This is a refutation of those who consider it invalid to pray behind a sinner.

Wiping over Leather Socks: A Sunnah with Quranic Evidence
Describes the ruling and provides dual evidence from Quranic readings and prophetic practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ruling of wiping over leather socks is established as a sunnah through both the Quranic variant readings and the Prophet's practice.

Core Idea
The Prophet's action resolves the Quranic ambiguity between washing and wiping.

This passage explores the basis for the ruling of wiping over leather socks (al-mash 'ala al-khuffayn). While many know it from the Sunnah, the author shows it can also be inferred from the Quran by examining the two canonical readings of verse 6 of Surah al-Ma'idah. The prophetic practice then clarifies which reading applies when wearing khuffayn, confirming the sunnah status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author defines the duration for resident and traveler, and then presents the evidence.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two Quranic readings
The author refers to the accusative (nasb) and genitive (jarr) readings of 'arjulakum'. Understand how each reading suggests a different ruling: washing vs. wiping.
These readings are from the seven canonical Qira'at.
3
See how the Prophet resolves the conflict
The conflicting readings leave the ruling ambiguous. The Prophet's action—wiping when wearing khuffayn, washing when uncovered—clarifies that both are valid in their respective contexts.
This shows that the Sunnah explains the Quran.
4
Reflect on the interplay of Quran and Sunnah
The passage demonstrates that the ruling is not solely from hadith; the Quran also provides evidence when read with the Sunnah. Consider how this method of inference works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمسح على الخفين أي للمقيم يوما وليلة وللمسافر ثلاثة أيام بلياليها (سنة) أي ثابت بالسنة التي كادت أن تكون متواترة، ولا يبعد أن يؤخذ ثبوته من الكتاب أيضاً؛ لأن قوله تعالى: وَأَرْجُلَكُمْ إِلَى الْكَعْبَيْنِ ﴾ [المائدة : ٦] ، قريء بالنصب في السبعة الأظهر في الغسل، والجر الأظهر في المسح وهما متعارضان، وبحسب الحكم مبهمان فبينهما فعل رسول الله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم حيث مسحهما حال لبس الخفين وغسلهما عند كشف الرجلين.
And wiping over the leather socks (al-khuffayn) means: for the resident one day and one night, and for the traveler three days and their nights. (Sunnah) meaning established by the Sunnah which nearly reached the level of mutawatir. And it is not far-fetched that its proof is also taken from the Book, because His statement the Exalted: 'and your feet to the ankles' [al-Ma'idah 6] was recited with the accusative (nasb) in the seven [readings] which is the apparent case for washing, and with the genitive (jarr) which is the apparent case for wiping, and they are conflicting, and in terms of ruling they are ambiguous. So the action of the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) clarified between them, as he wiped them when wearing the leather socks and washed them when the feet were uncovered.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling of wiping over leather socks (al-mash 'ala al-khuffayn) as a sunnah. It begins by specifying the duration: for a resident, one day and one night; for a traveler, three days and their nights. The author explains that 'sunnah' here means established by the Prophetic practice that is nearly mutawatir (massively transmitted). He then argues that the evidence for this practice can also be derived from the Quran by considering the two well-known readings of verse 6 of Surah al-Ma'idah. The word 'arjulakum' is recited in the accusative case, which is the apparent form for washing, and in the genitive case, which is the apparent form for wiping. These two readings seem to conflict, and the legal ruling derived from them alone is ambiguous. Therefore, the actual practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) clarifies the intended ruling: he would wipe over his socks when wearing them and wash his feet when uncovered. Thus, the passage demonstrates that the ruling is grounded in both the Qur'an and the Sunnah, reconciling the apparent textual ambiguity through the prophetic action.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the evidence for wiping over khuffayn is only from hadith, not from the Quran.
Correction: The author shows that the Quran provides evidence through the variant readings, which together with the Sunnah confirm the ruling.

Checkpoint

A) The accusative reading indicates washing, the genitive indicates wiping, and the Prophet's practice resolved the ambiguity.
B) The accusative reading indicates wiping, the genitive indicates washing.
C) Both readings indicate wiping.
D) The Quran does not provide evidence, only the Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over leather socks is a sunnah supported by both the Quran (through variant readings) and the Prophet's clarifying practice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Concessions for Travel: Shortening Prayer and Breaking Fast
Explains the Quranic basis and ruling for travel concessions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic evidence for the travel concessions of shortening prayer and breaking fast, and how the ruling differs between them.

Core Idea
Shortening prayer is an obligatory concession; breaking fast is optional but recommended.

Travel brings two major concessions: shortening the four-rak'ah prayers and breaking the fast during Ramadan. The Quran explicitly mentions both, but their legal force differs. This passage from a classical fiqh text clarifies the evidence and the correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal English translation. Notice how the author presents two separate concessions with different Quranic verses.
The passage uses technical terms like القصر (shortening) and الإفطار (breaking fast).
2
Identify the two concessions
The passage first states that both concessions are established by the Quran. Then it quotes the verse for shortening prayer and for breaking fast. Note the verses: 4:101 and 2:184.
3
Understand the ruling for shortening prayer
The author explains that the concession for shortening prayer is 'obligatory to act upon' based on a hadith. A traveler who prays four rak'ahs is doing wrong (musi').
The hadith calls it a sadaqah (charity) from Allah, which must be accepted.
4
Understand the ruling for breaking fast
The author notes that the concession for breaking fast is not as clear. The Zahiri school considered it obligatory to break fast, but the passage argues that the permissibility is derived from the end of the verse and other reports.
The phrase 'And that you fast is better for you' indicates that fasting is permissible but not required.
5
Contrast the two rulings
Notice the difference: one concession is mandatory to take, the other is optional. Reflect on why the rulings differ based on the textual evidence.
This highlights the importance of understanding the strength of legal evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال فيه، والقصر والإفطار رخصة في حالة السفر بنص الكتاب، ففي القصر قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا ضَرَبْتُمْ فِي الْأَرْضِ فَلَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ أَنْ تَقْصُرُ وا مِنَ الصَّلوة ﴾ [النساء : ۱۰۱] ، وفي الإفطار قوله تعالى: ﴿ فَمَنْ كَانَ مِنْكُمْ مَّرِيضًا أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَة [البقرة: ١٨٤]، انتهى. والرخصة في الآية الأولى واجبة العمل لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ((صدقة تصدق الله بها عليكم فاقبلوا صدقته)) . (۱) ولهذا لو صلى المسافر أربعا يكون مسيئا، وأما الرخصة في الآية الثانية غير ظاهرة بحسب الدلالة بل الظاهرية ذهبوا إلى وجوب ترك الصوم هنالك وقضائه بعد ذلك، وإنما الرخصة مستفادة من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاَنْ تَصُومُوا خَيْرٌ لَكُمْ إِن كُنتُمْ تَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ [البقرة : ١٨٤] الأخبار التي تثبت جواز الإفطار في الأسفار.
Then he said in it: 'And shortening [the prayer] and breaking the fast are concessions (rukhsa) in the state of travel by the text of the Book. As for shortening, the Exalted said: "And when you travel in the land, there is no blame upon you for shortening the prayer" [al-Nisa': 101]. And as for breaking the fast, the Exalted said: "So whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days" [al-Baqarah: 184]. End. The concession in the first verse is obligatory to act upon due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'A charity that Allah has given as charity to you, so accept His charity.' Hence, if a traveler prays four [rak'ahs], he is doing wrong. As for the concession in the second verse, it is not apparent in terms of indication, rather the Zahiris went to the obligation of leaving the fast there and making it up afterwards, and the concession is only derived from His saying: "And that you fast is better for you, if you only knew" [al-Baqarah: 184] and the reports that affirm the permissibility of breaking the fast in travels.'
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage addresses two legal concessions (rukhas) related to travel: shortening the prayer (qasr) and breaking the fast (iftaar). It begins by stating that these are established by the Quranic text (nass al-kitab). For qasr, the verse from al-Nisa' (4:101) is cited: there is no sin in shortening the prayer when traveling. For iftaar, the verse from al-Baqarah (2:184) allows the sick and traveler to break the fast and make up days later. The author then explains that the concession for shortening prayer is 'obligatory to act upon' (wajibat al-'amal) based on a hadith: the Prophet (sallallahu 'alayhi wa sallam) called it a sadaqah (charity) from Allah, and we must accept His charity. Therefore, if a traveler prays the full four rak'ahs, he is at fault (musi'). As for the fast-breaking concession, the author notes that it is not as clear in its dalalah (indication) as the first. The Zahiri school (al-Zahiriyya) held that it is obligatory to break the fast when traveling and make up later. However, the author argues that the concession is derived from the second part of the same verse: 'And that you fast is better for you' which implies that fasting is permissible but not obligatory, and from other reports (akhbar) that affirm the permissibility of breaking fast during travel. This clarifies the Sunni position that both concessions are valid, but the first is more emphatic as an obligation to take the concession, while the second is optional: one may fast or break, though the default for travel is to break.

Key Terms
القصر
الإفطار
رخصة
الظاهرية
قصر الصلاة في السفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that shortening prayer is always optional, similar to breaking fast.
Correction: In the Shafi'i school (and many others), shortening prayer for a traveler is an emphatic sunnah or even obligatory (wajib al-'amal), while breaking fast is merely permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a traveler who prays four rak'ahs instead of shortening?
A) It is better to pray full
B) He is doing wrong (musi')
C) It is invalid
D) He must repeat in qada

Key Takeaway
Shortening prayer is an obligatory concession when traveling, while breaking fast is a permissible concession that may be abandoned if one is able to fast.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you travel, do you willingly shorten your prayers as an act of accepting Allah's charity, or do you feel guilty or less spiritual for doing so? What does that say about your view of Allah's mercy?
?
The Prophet described shortening prayer as a charity from Allah—do you treat it as a gift to be eagerly accepted, or do you sometimes reject it by praying full? What holds you back from accepting this concession?
?
When you travel during Ramadan, how do you decide between fasting and breaking your fast? Is your decision based on genuine hardship and closeness to Allah, or on convenience and desire? How can you align your choice with the spirit of the concession?

Maturidi Method: Revelation First, Reason in Defense
Fakhr al-Razi attributes to Maturidi a two-step method: revealed proofs first, rational defense second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi methodological approach: prioritizing revealed proofs while using rational argumentation defensively.

Core Idea
Revelation leads; reason defends.

Fakhr al-Razi here attributes to Imam al-Maturidi a balanced method for theological debate. The primary reliance is on transmitted proofs (sam'iyyat), but when opponents raise doubts, rational arguments are used for repulsion and refutation. This lesson unpacks that method and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two-part structure: first, using revealed proofs; second, countering doubts with rational proofs.
2
Identify the two roles of reason
Reason is not primary for establishing doctrine but is used defensively. Notice the terms 'daf' and 'radd' (repulsion and refutation).
3
Reflect on the audience
Why does Maturidi say revealed proofs are 'clearer for the common people' and 'swifter in compelling opponents'? Think about accessibility and persuasion.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the quiz question to confirm you understand the primary basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفخر الرازي: مذهبنا في هذه المسألة ما اختاره الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي: أن نتمسك بالدلائل السمعية في إثبات مذهبنا، فإنه أسرع في إلزام الخصوم وأظهر في تفهيم العوام، وإذا ذكر الخصوم شبهتهم على هذه الدلائل النقلية نعارضهم بالمعقول على وجه الدفع والردّ.
Fakhr al-Razi said: Our madhhab in this issue is what Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi chose: that we hold fast to the transmitted proofs in establishing our madhhab, for it is swifter in compelling the opponents and clearer in making the common people understand. And if the opponents mention their doubt against these transmitted proofs, we counter them with the rational [proofs] for the purpose of repulsion and refutation.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement by Fakhr al-Razi, attributing to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi the approach for dealing with the beatific vision (and similar issues). The method is: first, rely on transmitted proofs (al-dala'il al-sam'iyya), i.e., Quran and hadith, to establish the doctrine. This is said to be more effective in compelling opponents and clearer for the common people. However, if the opponents raise doubts (shubuh) against these transmitted proofs, then the Maturidis counter them using rational arguments (al-ma'qul) for the purpose of repulsion (daf') and refutation (radd). This shows that reason is used defensively, not as a primary source of doctrine. It is a balanced approach that prioritizes revelation but does not shy from rational argumentation when needed.

Key Terms
دلائل سمعية
معقول
دفع
ردّ
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidis rely solely on reason.
Correction: The passage shows the primary reliance is on revealed proofs; reason is only used defensively.

Checkpoint
According to Fakhr al-Razi, what is the primary basis for establishing the madhhab in this issue?
A) Rational proofs
B) Revealed proofs (sam'iyyat)
C) Consensus of the community
D) Personal opinion

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi method uses revealed proofs as the foundation and reason only for defense against doubts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Reflect on your daily habits: do you seek knowledge from revealed sources (Quran and Sunnah) as your foundation, or do you build your beliefs on what 'makes sense' to you? What practical step can you take today to make the transmitted proofs the starting point of your learning?

The Maturidi Method of Proof: Scripture and Reason
Al-Razi states the Maturidi method of using transmitted proofs and countering doubts with rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi school's balanced approach of using transmitted proofs as primary evidence and reason as a defensive tool against objections.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs lead; reason defends.

Al-Razi summarizes the Maturidi method in a single, precise statement. He explains why the school prioritizes transmitted proofs and how it handles rational objections. This lesson unpacks this methodological principle and its implications for theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Let the author's statement settle.
Notice the concise style: a single sentence contains the whole method.
2
Identify the two components
The passage has two parts: reliance on transmitted proofs and the role of reason. Mark them.
Transmitted proofs (dalail sam'iyya) are for establishing doctrine; reason (ma'qul) is for rebuttal.
3
Understand the reasons for transmitted proofs
Two reasons are given: it is faster in compelling opponents and clearer for the common people. Reflect on why these matter.
This shows a practical and pedagogical concern.
4
Recognize the defensive role of reason
Reason is used to counter doubts (shubahat) raised against transmitted proofs. It is a shield, not a foundation.
The phrase 'ala wajh al-daf' wa al-radd' means 'in the way of rebuttal and rejection'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفخر الرازي: مذهبنا في هذه المسألة ما اختاره الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي: أن نتمسك بالدلائل السمعية في إثبات مذهبنا، فإنه أسرع في إلزام الخصوم وأظهر في تفهيم العوام، وإذا ذكر الخصوم شبهتهم على هذه الدلائل النقلية نعارضهم بالمعقول على وجه الدفع والردّ.
Al-Fakhr al-Razi said: Our school in this matter is what Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi chose: that we cling to transmitted evidences (dalail sam'iyya) in establishing our doctrine, for it is swifter in compelling opponents and clearer in informing the common people; and when the opponents mention their doubts concerning these transmitted proofs, we counter them with rational arguments (ma'qul) in the way of rebuttal and rejection.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents a methodological statement attributed to Fakhr al-Razi regarding the Maturidi approach to proving the vision of Allah (and other theological matters). The author defines the Maturidi method as relying primarily on transmitted evidences (al-dalail al-sam'iyya), i.e., scripture and revelation, when establishing the doctrine. He gives two reasons: first, it is more effective in compelling opponents (as they cannot deny their own scriptures), and second, it is clearer for teaching the common people. However, when opponents raise intellectual doubts against these transmitted proofs, the Maturidi school does not abandon reason but counters them with rational arguments (al-ma'qul) for the purpose of rebuttal and rejection. This shows a balanced approach: scripture is the primary source, but reason is used defensively to protect revealed truths.

Key Terms
الدلائل السمعية (al-dalail al-sam'iyya)
المعقول (al-ma'qul)
الإلزام (ilzam)
التفهيم (tafhīm)
الدفع والرد (al-daf' wa al-radd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi school rejects reason entirely and relies only on scripture.
Correction: This passage shows that reason is used defensively: when opponents raise doubts against transmitted proofs, the Maturidi school counters them with rational arguments for rebuttal and rejection.

Checkpoint

A) Because reason is unreliable.
B) Because it is quicker in compelling opponents and clearer for the common people.
C) Because transmitted proofs are easier to find.
D) Because rational arguments are forbidden.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi method prioritizes transmitted proofs for establishing doctrine but uses reason defensively to counter objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt about your faith, do you instinctively turn to reason or to scripture first? Which source do you truly trust as your foundation? This week, choose one doubt and deliberately answer it by first citing a clear verse or hadith before using any rational explanation.
?
Have you ever prioritized 'what makes sense to me' over a clear teaching from revelation without realizing it? Identify one area where your reasoning might have subtly overruled scripture. Will you commit to re-examining that area using the Maturidi method—starting with transmitted proofs?
?
Think of a person you know who struggles with religious doubt. When you speak to them, do you lead with rational arguments or with the simple words of Allah and His Messenger? How can you change your approach to be more like the Maturidi method—clear, compassionate, and grounded in revelation? Make a concrete plan to reach out to them this week.

The Obligation to Seek Knowledge and the Ruling of Stopping
Ruling on seeking detailed knowledge and the consequence of stopping.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why delaying the pursuit of detailed knowledge of Allah's attributes is not permitted and why indefinite suspension of judgment (waqf) is considered disbelief.

Core Idea
Stopping (waqf) on essential beliefs is disbelief because it causes doubt.

This passage from the sharh of al-Fiqh al-Akbar addresses a crucial methodological point: once a believer encounters uncertainty about a divine attribute, they must seek knowledge immediately. The text explains that failing to do so—especially by indefinitely suspending judgment—amounts to disbelief. This is not a minor oversight but a serious failure in tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the structure: the duty to ask, the prohibition of delay, and the ruling of disbelief for stopping.
The parentheses in the Arabic indicate interlinear commentary; the core text is what appears in parentheses.
2
Identify the three actions described
Find the three actions: (1) the person must ask, (2) delaying is not permissible, (3) stopping leads to immediate disbelief.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the ruling
The text explicitly states that stopping causes doubt, and doubt in matters of obligatory belief is equivalent to denial. Reflect on why doubt is so serious.
This connects to the learning objective about the gravity of waqf.
4
Consider the example of al-Thalji
The passage mentions a scholar who took a neutral stance on the Qur'an's createdness. Note how his evasive position was rejected because it implied doubt.
This clarifies that neutralizing creed is not acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيسأله ولا يسعه تأخير الطلب ولا يعذر بالوقف فيه، ويكفر إن وقف، عارفا بحقيقة الأحوال (فيسأله) أي ليعلم العلم التفصيلي على وجه الكمال، ولا يسعه تأخير الطلب أي عند تردّده في صفة من صفات الجلال أو نعوت الجمال، ولا يعذر بالوقف فيه) أي بتوقفه في معرفة هذه الأحوال وعدم تفحصه بالسؤال (ويكفر) أي في الحال إن (وقف) أي بأن توقف على بيان الأمر في الاستقبال؛ لأن التوقف موجب للشك وهو فيما يفترض اعتقاده كالإنكار، ولذا أبطلوا قول الثلجي من أصحابنا حيث قال : أقول بالمتفق، وهو أنه كلامه تعالى ولا أقول مخلوق أو قديم.
So let him ask, and it is not permissible for him to delay the request, and he is not excused for stopping in it, and he becomes a disbeliever if he stops. (Commentary:) Aware of the reality of the states, ("So let him ask") means to learn the detailed knowledge perfectly, and it is not permissible for him to delay the request, meaning when he hesitates regarding an attribute of majesty or beauty, and he is not excused for stopping in it, meaning by his stopping in knowing these states and his not investigating by asking. ("And he becomes a disbeliever") means immediately if he stops, meaning by suspending (judgment) until future clarification; because stopping causes doubt, and doubt regarding what is obligatory to believe is like denial. Hence they invalidated the statement of al-Thalji from our companions who said: I say according to what is agreed upon, namely that it is His speech, and I do not say created or eternal.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines the obligation to seek detailed knowledge of Allah's attributes and the ruling on stopping (waqf). The author explains that if a person is in doubt about an attribute of majesty or beauty, they must seek knowledge immediately. Delaying is not permissible. Furthermore, if one stops (i.e., suspends judgment indefinitely), it is instantaneous disbelief. The reasoning is that stopping leads to doubt, and doubt in matters of obligatory belief is equivalent to denial. The author then supports this by noting that the scholars rejected the view of al-Thalji, who avoided declaring whether the Qur'an is created or eternal, saying only 'it is His speech' without further qualification, because such evasiveness implies doubt.

Key Terms
توقف (waqf)
شك (doubt)
إنكار (denial)
تفصيل (detail)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Suspending judgment on theological matters (waqf) is a cautious, acceptable stance.
Correction: The passage declares that waqf is disbelief when it pertains to essential attributes, because it entails doubt, which is equivalent to denial.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on stopping (waqf) regarding the attributes of Allah?
A) Permissible if done cautiously
B) Disbelief (kufr) because it leads to doubt
C) Excused if one is ignorant
D) Equivalent to a sin but not disbelief

Key Takeaway
Stopping (tawaqquf) on essential beliefs is disbelief because it entails doubt, and doubt is equivalent to denial in matters of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any attribute of Allah or matter of belief that you have deliberately avoided investigating because it feels uncomfortable or uncertain? What is your excuse before Allah today, knowing that hesitation itself can be disbelief?
?
You may claim to believe in tawhid, but do you truly seek detailed knowledge of His attributes, or do you settle for vague generalities? What does your pattern of avoiding difficult questions say about the state of your iman?
?
If you were asked right now about the Qur'an—is it created or eternal?—could you answer with certainty, or would you hesitate? And what does that hesitation reveal about the strength of your conviction? Commit today to resolve any ambiguity until your heart is firm.

The Critical Distinction Between Errors in Fiqh and Kalam
Distinguishes between errors in fiqh, which are forgiven, and errors in kalam, which are disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical distinction between theological error and jurisprudential error, and why only the former can constitute disbelief.

Core Idea
Error in theology is disbelief; error in fiqh is forgiven and rewarded.

In Islamic theology, not all mistakes are equal. This passage from Sanusi's commentary clarifies a foundational principle: disagreements in matters of belief (tawhid) lead to misguidance, while disagreements in practical rulings (fiqh) are considered a mercy. This distinction shapes how scholars view error and innovation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author uses contrasting pairs: mercy vs misguidance, forgiven vs disbelief.
2
Identify the two domains
The author distinguishes 'subtleties of tawhid' from the 'rulings of Islam'. Mark which domain each belongs to and what the consequence of doubt or error is.
Tawhid deals with Allah's essence and attributes; fiqh deals with practical laws.
3
Note the consequences of error
For fiqh: error is forgiven and the scholar is rewarded. For kalam: error is disbelief and the scholar is sinful. This is a stark contrast.
The author uses the terms 'maghfoor' (forgiven) and 'kufr' (disbelief).
4
Reflect on why this distinction matters
Ask yourself: Why does theology have such strict boundaries? How does this affect the way Muslims approach disagreements in belief versus practice?
This distinction protects the core of Islamic faith while allowing flexibility in law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا، والمراد بدقائق علم التوحيد أشياء يكون الشك والشبهة فيها منافيا للإيمان ومناقضا للإيقان بذات الله تعالى وصفته ومعرفة كيفية المؤمن به بأحوال آخرته، فلا ينافي أن الإمام توقف في بعض الأحكام؛ لأنها في شرائع الإسلام، فالاختلاف في علم الأحكام رحمة والاختلاف في علم التوحيد والإسلام ضلالة ،وبدعة والخطأ في علم الأحكام مغفور بل صاحبه فيه مأجور ، بخلاف الخطأ في علم الكلام، فإنه كفر وزور صاحبه مأزور.
This, and what is meant by the subtleties of the science of tawhid are things in which doubt and suspicion are contrary to faith and contradictory to certainty in the Essence of Allah Almighty and His attributes, and knowledge of the state of the believer in the matters of the Hereafter. So it is not inconsistent that an imam might stop regarding some rulings, because they belong to the laws of Islam. Thus, disagreement in the science of rulings is a mercy, while disagreement in the science of tawhid and Islam is misguidance and innovation; and error in the science of rulings is forgiven — rather its practitioner is rewarded — unlike error in the science of kalam, for it is disbelief and falsehood, and its practitioner is sinful.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between two types of knowledge: the subtleties of tawhid (divine unity) and the rulings of fiqh. The author states that the 'subtleties of tawhid' are matters where doubt is incompatible with faith and certainty in Allah's essence, attributes, and the afterlife. Therefore, it is not problematic if an imam (scholar) suspends judgment on some fiqh rulings, because those belong to the laws of Islam. The author then presents a key principle: disagreement in fiqh is a mercy (meaning it is allowed and can be fruitful), whereas disagreement in tawhid leads to misguidance and innovation. Errors in fiqh are forgiven, and the one who errs is rewarded (because he intended to deduce the ruling). In contrast, error in kalam (theology) is considered disbelief (kufr) and falsehood, and the one who errs is sinful. This underscores the seriousness of theological mistakes compared to jurisprudential ones.

Key Terms
دقائق علم التوحيد (daka'iq al-tawhid)
اختلاف (ikhtilaf)
رحمة (mercy)
ضلالة (misguidance)
بدعة (innovation)
مغفور (forgiven)
مأجور (rewarded)
مأزور (sinful)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that disagreement in theology is just as permissible as disagreement in fiqh.
Correction: This passage shows that theological disagreement is misguidance and innovation, not a mercy. Error in kalam is disbelief, while error in fiqh is forgiven and rewarded.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between error in fiqh and error in kalam?
A) Both are forgiven
B) Error in fiqh is kufr, error in kalam is sin
C) Error in kalam is kufr, error in fiqh is forgiven and rewarded
D) Both are misguidance and innovation

Key Takeaway
Theological error leads to disbelief, while jurisprudential error is forgiven and rewarded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a theological belief you hold that you have never seriously questioned. Could it be that you hold it merely as an inherited opinion, not as the certain knowledge (yaqin) demanded by the scholars of tawhid? If that belief turned out to be erroneous, would it compromise your faith? What will you do this week to examine it against the clear sources?
?
When you encounter a difference of opinion in fiqh (e.g., ruling on prayertime), you may accept it easily. But when you hear a disagreement in a matter of aqeedah (e.g., attributes of Allah), does it disturb you? This passage says such disagreement is misguidance and innovation. Does your heart feel the weight of that? Or have you become comfortable with theological confusion? Repent and resolve to seek only the truth in matters of faith.
?
The passage declares that error in theology is not just a mistake—it is kufr. Have you ever thought of a theological error as 'just a minor point'? Now that you know the gravity, what changes will you make in how you learn and teach matters of tawhid? Will you commit to verifying every belief through the Quran and authentic Sunnah, even if it upsets what you were raised to believe?

Special Guidance and the Imam's Example
Commentator distinguishes special guidance from general invitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between Allah's general invitation to all and His special guidance of the elect, and why the Great Imam concluded his creed with this special guidance.

Core Idea
Allah's special guidance is a pure gift, not a general invitation.

In this passage, the commentator reflects on the verse "And Allah guides whom He wills to a straight path." He distinguishes between two kinds of divine calling: the general invitation extended to all people by justice, and the special, pure guidance granted to some by grace. The Great Imam Abu Hanifa chose to end his creed with this special guidance, teaching us to pray for a good end and divine protection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation provided. Notice the two kinds of guidance mentioned: one by justice (general invitation) and one by grace (special guidance).
The verse quoted (Yunus:25) is the general invitation.
2
Identify the contrast
The commentator contrasts 'guidance by grace' with 'invitation by justice'. Understand that special guidance is not for everyone; it is a pure gift from Allah.
Reflect on why the Imam emphasized this in his creed.
3
Apply the lesson
The Imam's example shows we should seek a good end (husn al-khatima) through reliance on divine care and protection. We emulate him by maintaining our faith from beginning to end.
The passage ends with a prayer for pardon, well-being, and perpetual care.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والله يهدي من يشاء إلى صراط مستقيم أي من جمال فضله وإن كان سبحانه كما قال: ﴿ وَاللهُ يَدْعُوا إِلى دَارِ السَّالِمِ ﴾ [يونس : ٢٥] عموم الأنام بمقتضى عدله، فختم الإمام الأعظم معتقده بالهداية الخاصة الخالصة، فنقتدي به في طلب حسن الخاتمة باستمرار حالة البداية إلى مقام النهاية، مقرونا بعين العناية وزين الحماية عما يؤدي إلى الضلالة والغواية، فنسأل الله العفو والعافية، ودوام الرعاية.
And Allah guides whom He wills to a straight path, meaning from the beauty of His grace, even though He, exalted is He, as He said: {And Allah invites to the abode of peace} [Yunus: 25] to all people by virtue of His justice. So the Great Imam concluded his creed with the special, pure guidance, so we emulate him in seeking a good end by continuing the state of the beginning to the station of the end, accompanied by the eye of care and the ornament of protection from what leads to misguidance and error. So we ask Allah for pardon, well-being, and perpetual care.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is an interpretive commentary on the verse 'And Allah guides whom He wills to a straight path.' The commentator explains that 'guides' here refers to the beauty of Allah's grace, not as a general invitation, which is for all people by His justice as in the verse 'And Allah invites to the abode of peace.' The Great Imam (Abu Hanifa) concluded his creed with this special pure guidance, and the commentator says we should follow his example in seeking a good end by maintaining the state we had at the beginning (of faith) until the end, with divine care and protection from misguidance. The commentator ends with a prayer for pardon, well-being, and perpetual care.

Key Terms
الهداية الخاصة
حسن الخاتمة
العناية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that divine guidance is only general, i.e., Allah invites everyone equally without distinction.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that while Allah does invite all people by His justice, He also grants special, pure guidance to whom He wills by His grace. This special guidance is distinct and not automatic for everyone.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say the Imam concluded his creed with?
A) General invitation
B) Special pure guidance
C) A prayer for the afterlife
D) A refutation of the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
The Great Imam concluded his creed with the special guidance of Allah, reminding us that our good end depends on His grace and protection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life, do you rely more on your own efforts to secure a good end, or do you truly depend on Allah's special grace and protection? What would change if you lived with the constant awareness that your final state is entirely in His hands?
?
The Great Imam concluded his creed with this special guidance. In your daily life, what is the one thing you are most tempted to think you control regarding your faith? Will you surrender that area to Allah's care and protection today?
?
If you were to die right now, would you be certain that your end is good? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's grace versus your own deeds? What concrete step can you take today to seek His perpetual care?
